Effect of Milk from Zina on Fosterage Prohibitions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zina milk's prohibition scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on milk from zina and apply the preferred view to determine whether a foster daughter can marry the adulterer's son.

Core Idea
Milk from zina is disputed: some say full prohibition, others only from mother's side until paternity is proven.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding fosterage from milk produced through adultery. The source passage from 'Ikhtiyar fi Sharh al-Mukhtar' discusses two positions and their practical consequences, particularly concerning who becomes mahram to the nursing child. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh conflicting analogies within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
What is the act being ruled on? A woman who has committed zina and then breastfeeds a child. Does this create the same prohibitions as lawful fosterage? Identify the two views mentioned.
The dispute is about the scope of prohibition, not its existence.
2
State the first view and its dalil
The first view equates milk from zina to lawful milk. What is their evidence? They analogize to a child born from zina, who is mahram only to direct ascendants/descendants due to shubhat al-juz’iyya (semblance of partial lineage).
This analogy restricts prohibition to the adulterer and his direct line, not collateral relatives.
3
State the second view (al-Webari) and its tarjih
Al-Webari holds that prohibition from zina milk is initially only from the mother's side. The father's side becomes relevant only if paternity is legally established. Fath al-Qadir preferred this view.
This view is more cautious and relies on the lack of confirmed lineage from the adulterer.
4
Apply the preferred view to a scenario
A woman commits zina, becomes pregnant, gives birth, and later breastfeeds a baby girl. According to the preferred view, can that girl marry the adulterer's son? Answer: Not until the adulterer's paternity is proven; initially the girl is only mahram to the woman's relatives.
The practical consequence depends on whether lineage is established.
5
Note the author's additional fatwa
The author (the commentator) mentions he wrote a detailed fatwa defending his stance against someone who disputed him. This shows that even within the Hanafi school, such issues were debated and resolved via authoritative tarjih.
This reinforces the importance of later Hanafi authorities like Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1] قوله : وزوجها فيه إشارة عدم حرمة الزاني عليه، وكذا أقربائه، وقـد اختلف فيه، فقيل : لبن الزنا كالحلال، فإذا أرضعت به بنتاً حرمت على الزاني وآبائه وأبنائه والعمّ الزاني وأخيه التزوّج بها، كالمولودة من الزنا ; فإنّها تحرم على الزاني وأصوله وفروعه بشبهة الجزئية، ولا تحرم على العم والأخ.
(۳) (٤)
وذكر الوبري : إنّ الحرمة تثبت من جهة الأم خاصة، ما لم يثبت النسب فحينئذ تثبت من جهة الأب، ورجحه في «فتح القدير ) ، وقد بسطت الكلام في هذه المسألة في بعض فتاواي ردّاً على من نازعني فيها وأتى بما أتى.
[1] His statement: 'and her husband' contains an indication of the absence of prohibition upon the adulterer, and likewise his relatives. And there is difference about it. Some said: the milk of zina is like lawful milk; so if a girl is suckled with it, she becomes forbidden to the adulterer, his fathers, his sons, the adulterous paternal uncle and his brother in marriage, like a child born from zina; for she is forbidden to the adulterer and his ancestors and his descendants due to the semblance of partiality, but she is not forbidden to the paternal uncle or brother. Al-Webari mentioned that the prohibition is established from the mother's side specifically, unless lineage is proven, then it is established from the father's side. And he (al-Webari) preferred this in Fath al-Qadir. And I (the author) have elaborated on this issue in some of my fatawa in response to one who disputed with me and brought various arguments.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether milk produced through adultery (zina) creates the same marriage prohibitions as milk from a lawful marriage. The note explains that the phrase 'and her husband' in the ruling implies that the adulterer (who is not the husband) is not prohibited, but scholars differ. One view equates zina milk to lawful milk: if a child suckles from a woman who conceived by zina, the child becomes mahram to the adulterer and his ascendants/descendants, but not to collateral relatives like uncle or brother—analogous to a child born from zina where only direct ascendants/descendants are forbidden due to a semblance of partiality. The second view, attributed to al-Webari and favoured in Fath al-Qadir, states that prohibition initially only applies from the mother's side (the nursing woman) until the adulterer's paternity is legally established—then it extends to his side. The author notes that he has written a detailed fatwa on this, defending his stance. The practical consequence: whether, for example, a foster daughter can marry the adulterer's son depends on which view is followed.

Key Terms
لبن الزنا (laban al-zinā): Milk produced by a woman from a pregnancy resulting from adultery.
شبهة الجزئية (shubhat al-juz’iyya): A semblance of partial paternity that creates limited prohibition (direct line) but not collateral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the prohibition from the father's side in al-Webari's view?
A) The mere fact that the child was nursed by a woman who committed zina
B) The proven lineage of the child from the father
C) The child being born from zina as opposed to lawful marriage
D) The agreement of all Hanafi scholars

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position on milk from zina is that prohibition is initially from the mother's side only, and the father's side becomes prohibitive only if his paternity is legally established.

Prohibition of Foster Child's Descendants and Spouse on Foster Family
Prohibition of foster child's descendants and spouse on foster family

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which relatives of the foster child become mahram to the foster family and explain the legal basis for this prohibition.

Core Idea
Fosterage mirrors lineage: the foster child’s descendants and spouse are haram on the foster parents, but his ascendants are not.

We now turn to the prohibitions arising from the foster child's side. Just as the foster mother and her husband become parents to the child, the child's own descendants become grandchildren, and his spouse becomes a daughter-in-law or son-in-law—all permanently prohibited for marriage. The commentator bases this on the principle of 'partiality of fosterage' (juz'iyyah rada'iyyah) and reinforces it with a Persian mnemonic. Note carefully that the foster child's biological parents are not included; only his downward line and one spouse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of prohibition from the foster child's side
The text mentions two sets: the foster child's descendants (furu') and the foster child's spouse. Both are prohibited on the foster mother and her husband. For the spouse, the direction depends on the gender of the foster child.
If foster child is male, his wife is prohibited on the foster mother's husband; if female, her husband is prohibited on the foster mother.
2
Understand the legal basis: juz'iyyah rada'iyyah
Commentary explains that when the foster child becomes a son/daughter to the foster parents through fosterage, his/her children become grandchildren to them. This establishes a mahram relationship like lineage.
The hadith 'Fosterage makes unlawful what lineage makes unlawful' underlies this.
3
Apply the analogy to descendants
Since foster child is like a child, his/her descendants are like grandchildren. Hence they are prohibited on the foster parents, just as biological grandchildren are.
This includes all levels of descendants, no matter how low.
4
Apply the analogy to spouse (musahara)
The foster child's spouse becomes like a daughter-in-law or son-in-law to the foster parents, prohibited by affinity (musahara).
This is specific: the foster child's spouse is prohibited to the foster parent of the opposite gender of the child.
5
Recognize the limitation: ascendants not included
The commentary explicitly states that the foster child's ascendants (biological parents) are not prohibited. Only the foster child's descendants and spouse are covered.
This matches lineage: one's own parents are not mahram to one's foster parents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحرم فروعُ الرّضيع على المرضعة وزوجها ، ويحرم زوج الرضيع على المرضعة وزوجها : أي الرّضيعُ إن كان ذكراً تحرم زوجته على زوج مرضعته ، وإن كان الرضيع أنثى يحرم زوجها على مرضعتها ، وضابطه "" ما في هذا البيت الفارسي : از جانب شیرده همه خویش شوند و ز جانب شیر خواره زوجان وفروع للابن المرتضع من أمّ البناتِ أن يتزوج واحدة منهن، وكان لأخوته أن يتزوجوا بنات الأخرى إلا الابنة التي أرضعتها أمهم وحدها ؛ لأنها أختهم من الرضاعة. كذا في «المبسوط»(۱) وبالجملة : يحرم قـوم المرضعة وزوجها أصولاً وفروعاً على الرضيع، فأولاد المرضعة كلّها تحرم على الرضيع، مقدّمة كانت على رضاعة أو مؤخّرة، وقس عليه. كذا في خزانة الروايات»، وغيره. [۱]قوله : وتحرم ... الخ ؛ ما ذكر سابقاً كان بياناً للحرمة من جانب المرضعة، وهذا بيان للحرمة من جانب الرضيع وحاصله : أن الرضيع تحرم أولاده وإن سفلوا على المرضعة وزوجها ؛ لثبوتِ الجزئية الرضاعيّة ؛ فإنّ الرضيعَ لَمَّا صار ابناً لهما بالرضاعة صارت أولاده أولادهما فتحرم عليهما. ولا تحرم أصوله عليهما ولا غيرهم من أقربائه، وكذا تثبت من جانب الحرمة الثابتة بالمصاهرة، فيحرم زوج الرضيعة على المرضعة، وتحرم زوجة الرضيع على زوج المرضعة ؛ لكونها حليلة ابنه الرضاعي، والتفصيل ليطلب من «فتح القدير» و«البحر الرائق». [٢] قوله : وضابطه ؛ أي ضابطةً ما ذكر من أقسام الحرمة من الجانبين هي ما يفيده هذا البيت ؛ فإنّ مفاد المصرع الأوّل أنّ من جانب المرضعة، وكذا زوجها يكون الكل ذا قرابة من الرضيع : أي الذين لهم قرابة محرمة من النسب، فيدخل فيه المرضعة وزوجها وأقربائهما، ومفاد المصرع الثاني أن من جانب الرضيع إنما يثبت القرابة للمرضعة وزوجها من فروعه وأحد زوجيه. (۱) «المبسوط» (۳۰: ۳۰۱).
And the descendants of the foster child are prohibited upon the foster mother and her husband, and the spouse of the foster child is prohibited upon the foster mother and her husband: i.e., if the foster child is a male, his wife is prohibited upon the husband of his foster mother, and if the foster child is a female, her husband is prohibited upon her foster mother. And its rule is what is in this Persian verse: 'From the side of the milk-giver, all become relatives, and from the side of the milk-drinker, the spouse and descendants.' For the foster son from among the daughters, he may marry one of them, and his brothers may marry the daughters of the other except the daughter who was suckled by their mother alone, because she is their sister through fosterage. Thus in al-Mabsut. In summary: the people of the foster mother and her husband, in terms of ascendants and descendants, are prohibited upon the foster child. So all children of the foster mother are prohibited upon the foster child, whether born before or after the fosterage. And analogize upon that. Thus in Khizanat al-Riwayat and others. [1] He said: 'And are prohibited...' etc. What was mentioned earlier was an explanation of the prohibition from the side of the foster mother. This is an explanation of the prohibition from the side of the foster child. Its summary: the children of the foster child, even if they descend, are prohibited upon the foster mother and her husband due to the establishment of the partiality of fosterage. For when the foster child becomes a son to them through fosterage, his children become their children, so they are prohibited upon them. And his ascendants are not prohibited upon them, nor other relatives of his. Similarly, it is established from the side of the prohibition established through affinity (musahara). So the husband of the foster daughter is prohibited upon the foster mother, and the wife of the foster son is prohibited upon the husband of the foster mother, because she is the wife of his foster son. The details are to be sought from Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. [2] He said: 'And its rule...' i.e., the rule for the categories of prohibition from both sides is what this verse indicates. For the meaning of the first hemistich is that from the side of the foster mother and her husband, everyone becomes a relative of the foster child, i.e., those who have a prohibitive relation by lineage. So it includes the foster mother, her husband, and their relatives. The meaning of the second hemistich is that from the side of the foster child, the relation is only established for the foster mother and her husband through his descendants and one of his spouses. (1) Al-Mabsut (30:301).
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah with its commentary. It states that the foster child's descendants (furu') are mahram on the foster mother and her husband, and likewise the foster child's spouse is mahram. The commentary explains that just as the foster mother and her husband become parents to the child through fosterage, so the child's own children become like grandchildren, and his spouse becomes like a daughter-in-law or son-in-law. The ruling is based on the principle of partiality (juz'iyyah rada'iyyah) established by the hadith 'Fosterage makes unlawful what lineage makes unlawful.' The commentary also provides a Persian verse as a mnemonic to summarize both sides: from the milk-giver's side, all become relatives; from the milk-drinker's side, only the spouse and descendants. Note the precision: ascendents of the foster child (e.g., his biological parents) are not included in this prohibition; only his descendants and one spouse. This matches the rules of lineage, where one's own children are mahram but one's parents are not (because they are from the other side).

Key Terms
الجزئية الرضاعية (al-juz'iyyah al-rada'iyyah): The legal principle that a child through fosterage becomes a partial 'part' (juz') of the foster family, establishing mahram relationships similar to lineage.
الرضيع (al-radee'): The child who is breastfed, i.e., the foster child.
المرضعة (al-murdi'ah): The woman who breastfeeds the child, i.e., the foster mother.
المصاهرة (al-musaharah): Affinity relationship established through marriage, which also creates marriage prohibitions. Here, applied to the foster child's spouse being considered like a daughter-in-law or son-in-law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, whose marriage is prohibited on the foster mother's husband?
A) The foster child's biological mother
B) The foster child's wife
C) The foster child's sister
D) The foster child's daughter

Key Takeaway
A foster child's descendants and spouse are permanently prohibited to the foster mother and her husband, but his biological parents are not included.

Sister of Foster Brother Is Halal for Marriage
Clarifies which relationships through fosterage are not prohibited

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the sister of a foster brother is halal to marry and explain the analogical reasoning from lineage relationships.

Core Idea
The sister of your foster brother is halal because fosterage mirrors lineage in prohibition scope.

It is common to think that once fosterage establishes a mahram relationship, all relatives of that person become mahram as well. This lesson clarifies the actual Hanafi ruling: only the specific relationships that are prohibited by lineage are also prohibited by fosterage. The sister of your foster brother does not fall into that category, so she is halal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is marrying the sister of one's foster brother. The ruling is that it is permissible (halal).
This is stated directly in the matn: 'وتحل أخت أخيه رضاعاً'.
2
Locate the general principle
The proof is the rule that only what is prohibited by lineage is prohibited by fosterage. The passage states: 'أن المحرم من الرضاع إنما هو ما يحرم من النسب'.
This principle is derived from the hadith: 'يحرم من الرضاع ما يحرم من النسب'.
3
Apply the analogy to a lineage scenario
In lineage, the sister of a paternal half-brother (from his mother's side) is halal. The passage gives the example of a brother from the father's side who has a maternal half-sister – she is halal for the other brother.
This shows that not all siblings of a sibling are mahram; only direct siblings are prohibited.
4
Extend to fosterage
Since the relationship in fosterage mirrors lineage, a foster brother's sister (whether through lineage or fosterage) is not mahram to you.
Three permutations are given: lineage brother with foster sister, foster brother with lineage sister, foster brother with foster sister.
5
Confirm consensus
The passage ends with 'اتفاقاً' (by consensus), indicating that there is no disagreement among scholars on this ruling.
This eliminates any doubt about differing opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحل أخت أخيه رضاعاً ، كما تحلُ نسباً : كاخ من الآب له أخت من أُمِّهِ تحل لأخيه من أبيه. (۱) قوله : وتحل أخت أخيه ؛ هذا بيان لما لا يحرم من الرضاع ولا من النسب. [۲] قوله : رضاعاً ؛ يصح اتصاله بالمضاف إليه وبالمضاف وبكليهما، كأن يكون لك أخ نسبي له أختُ رضاعيّة فهي حلال عليك، وكذا إذا كان لك أخ رضاعي له أخت نسبية، وكذا الأخت الرضاعية للأخ الرضاعي. والوجه فيه : أن المحرم من الرضاع إنما هو ما يحرم من النسب، ومثل هذه القرابة من النسب قد يكون غير محرّمة، فلا يحرم مثلها من الرضاع. (۳) قوله : كأخ من الأب ؛ صورته: أن يكون لك أخ من الأب ؛ أي يكون أبوك وأبوه واحد، أو أمك غير أمه، بأنّ تزوّج أبوك امرأتين إحداهما أمك والثانية أم أخيك، وكانت لأخيك المذكور أخت من الأم بأن كانت أمه متزوجة قبل أبيك بزوج آخر ، فولدت منه بنتاً فهي حلال عليك اتفاقاً.
And the sister of one's brother through fosterage is lawful, just as it is lawful through lineage: like a brother from the father's side who has a sister from his mother's side; she is lawful for his brother from the father's side. (1) He said: 'And the sister of one's brother is lawful...' This is an explanation of what is not prohibited through fosterage nor through lineage. [2] He said: 'through fosterage' — it is permissible to connect it to the mudaf ilayhi or to the mudaf or to both. For example, you have a lineage brother who has a foster sister; she is lawful for you. Similarly, if you have a foster brother who has a lineage sister, and similarly the foster sister of a foster brother. The point is that what is prohibited through fosterage is only what is prohibited through lineage, and such a relationship in lineage may be non-prohibiting, so its equivalent in fosterage is not prohibited. (3) He said: 'like a brother from the father's side' — its picture is that you have a brother from the father's side, i.e., your father and his father are the same, but your mother is not his mother. For example, your father married two women, one being your mother and the other being your brother's mother. And that brother had a sister from his mother's side, i.e., his mother was married before your father to another husband, and she gave birth to a daughter from that husband. So that daughter is lawful for you by consensus.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage clarifies that not every relationship through fosterage is prohibited. The general rule is that fosterage prohibits only those relationships that are prohibited by lineage. Since in lineage, the sister of a brother (who is not your own sister) is halal — e.g., a half-sister from the mother's side of your half-brother from the father's side — the same applies in fosterage. The commentary gives three permutations: a lineage brother having a foster sister, a foster brother having a lineage sister, and a foster brother having a foster sister. In all these cases, she is halal for you. The example illustrates: you have a half-brother from your father's side (same father, different mother). That half-brother has a half-sister from his mother's side (his mother's daughter from a previous husband). That woman is your half-brother's half-sister, but she is not your half-sister. She is halal for you to marry by consensus.

Key Terms
الرضاع (al-rada'): Fosterage through breastfeeding, which establishes certain marriage prohibitions.
أخ من الأب (akh min al-ab): A paternal half-brother, i.e., sharing the same father but different mother.
أخت من الأم (ukht min al-umm): A maternal half-sister, i.e., sharing the same mother but different father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
You have a foster brother (through your foster mother). He has a biological sister from his own parents. Can you marry her?
A) No, because she is your foster sister
B) Yes, because she is not your mahram through lineage or fosterage
C) No, because she is a relative of your foster brother
D) Only if she was also breastfed by your foster mother

Key Takeaway
The sister of a foster brother is halal to marry because fosterage prohibits only the same relationships as lineage, and a sibling's sibling is not prohibited in lineage.

Two Foster Children from the Same Breast: Mahram Siblingship
Ruling with expansion on fosterage prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the marriage prohibition between two children who suckled from the same woman, and identify the conditions under which they are considered full or half foster siblings.

Core Idea
Any two children from the same foster mother are like siblings and cannot marry, regardless of timing or the milk's biological father.

This lesson examines a specific ruling of fosterage (rada'): when two children are breastfed by the same woman, they become mahram to each other as siblings. The passage clarifies the absolute nature of this prohibition and explains how to classify the sibling relationship based on the lineage of the milk's source. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying fosterage prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is two children being breastfed by the same woman. The ruling is that marriage between them is haram because they are considered siblings through fosterage.
The prohibition is based on the principle that fosterage makes the woman a mother and her husband a father, as established in earlier sections.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is the general principle of rada' derived from Quran 4:23 and hadith. The text references this as already established.
The explicit dalil is not quoted here but is part of the foundation of the chapter.
3
Appreciate the absolute nature
The ruling applies whether the two children suckled at the same time or different times, and whether the milk came from one husband or multiple husbands. No condition of simultaneity or same husband is required.
This is a key expansion: the prohibition is unconditional with respect to time and paternity of the milk.
4
Classify the sibling type
If the milk came from one man (i.e., the husband of the foster mother at the time of both feedings), the children are like full siblings. If not, they are half-siblings through the mother (shiqaq min al-um) or father (min al-ab) depending on the scenario.
The commentary gives an example: a man with two wives, each breastfeeding a child from the other wife's pregnancy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورضيعا ثدي كأخ وأخت [4] قوله : ورضيعا ... الخ ؛ هذا مبتدأ خبره قوله : كأخ وأخت، وهذه المسألة وإن فهمت تما مر من أن الرضاعة تجعل المرضعة أُمّاً وزوجها أباً، لكن ذكرها على حدة توضيحاً وتمهيداً لذكر مسألة الشركة في لبن البهيمة، وفي إطلاق الرضيعين إشارة إلى إطلاق الحكم، فيحرم الرضيع على الرضيع الآخر، سواء اتحد زمان رضاعتهما أو اختلف، وسواء كان الزوج الذي لبنها منه واحد أو أكثر. [0]قوله : كأخ وأخت ; شقيقين إن كان اللبنُ الذي شرباه منها لرجل واحد، أو لأم إن لم يكن كذلك، ولأب بأن كان لرجل امرأتان وولدتا منه فأرضعت كلّ واحد غيراً).
And two sucklings of a breast are like a brother and a sister. [4] He said: 'And two sucklings...' this is a subject, its predicate is 'like a brother and a sister.' Although this issue is understood from what preceded — that fosterage makes the foster mother a mother and her husband a father — yet it is mentioned separately for clarification and as a prelude to mentioning the issue of partnership in the milk of an animal. And in the generality of 'two sucklings' is an indication of the generality of the ruling: so the one suckling is prohibited upon the other, whether their suckling times are the same or different, and whether the man whose milk it is (i.e., the husband) is one or more. [0] He said: 'like a brother and a sister' — full siblings if the milk they drank is from one man; or 'like a brother and a sister by mother' if not that; and 'like a brother and a sister by father' in the case where a man has two wives and they both give birth from him, and each breastfeeds someone else.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage establishes a general rule: any two children who have suckled from the same breast (i.e., from the same woman) become like brother and sister in terms of marriage prohibitions. This is derived from the principle that fosterage creates a partiality making the foster mother a mother and her husband a father. The commentary emphasizes that the ruling is absolute: it does not matter if the two children were breastfed at the same time or different times, nor does it matter if the milk came from the same husband or different husbands (since a woman's milk can come from different husbands in successive marriages). The final note clarifies the type of sibling relationship based on the source of milk: if the milk is from one man (i.e., the woman's husband is the biological father of both?), then they are like full siblings; otherwise, they are like half-siblings through the mother or father.

Key Terms
الرضيعان (al-radee'an): Two children who have been breastfed by the same woman; they become foster siblings.
الأخ والأخت (al-akh wa al-ukht): In this context, refers to foster siblings who are like biological siblings in terms of marriage prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, two children who suckled from the same breast are like:
A) Cousins and may marry
B) Siblings and cannot marry
C) Only half-siblings if the milk is from different husbands
D) Only forbidden if they are full siblings

Key Takeaway
Two children suckled by the same woman are siblings for marriage prohibition, absolutely and without condition regarding time or the husband's identity.

The Foster Brother's Sister is Halal
States and explains the ruling that sister of foster brother is halal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying the sister of a foster brother and explain the legal principle that fosterage mirrors lineage prohibitions.

Core Idea
Fosterage prohibits only what blood prohibits; what is halal in lineage is also halal in fosterage.

Many assume that any foster relationship creates a general brotherhood that blocks marriage. This lesson clarifies that fosterage only mirrors specific blood prohibitions. If a woman would be halal in blood (like a half-sister through a different father), her foster counterpart is also halal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Find the core ruling: 'the sister of a foster brother is permissible.' Notice the language 'تحل' (becomes permissible).
The ruling is positive (halal), not negative.
2
Locate the parallel with lineage
The author states that fosterage prohibitions mirror lineage prohibitions. Identify the phrase 'ما يحرم من النسب' (what is prohibited by lineage).
This is the key principle (al-dalil).
3
Analyse the example
Work through the example: you have a half-brother from father; he has a half-sister from mother (different father). In blood, she is not your mahram. Therefore, her foster equivalent is also not your mahram.
The example shows the application of the principle.
4
Summarize the proof
The proof is the general rule that fosterage only prohibits what blood prohibits. This is based on the hadith: 'Fosterage prohibits what is prohibited by lineage.'
No specific hadith is quoted here, but it is the underlying evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحل أخت أخيه رضاعاً ، كما تحلُّ نسباً : كاخ من الآب له أخت من أُمِّهِ تحل لأخيه من أبيه. قوله : وتحل أخت أخيه؛ هذا بيان لما لا يحرم من الرضاع ولا من النسب. قوله : رضاعاً؛ يصح اتصاله بالمضاف إليه وبالمضاف وبكليهما، كأن يكون لك أخ نسبي له أخت رضاعيّة فهي حلال عليك، وكذا إذا كان لك أخ رضاعي له أخت نسبية، وكذا الأخت الرضاعية للأخ الرضاعي. والوجه فيه : أن المحرم من الرضاع إنما هو ما يحرم من النسب، ومثل هذه القرابة من النسب قد يكون غير محرّمة، فلا يحرم مثلها من الرضاع. قوله : كأخ من الأب؛ صورته: أن يكون لك أخ من الأب؛ أي يكون أبوك وأبوه واحد، أو أمك غير أمه، بأنّ تزوّج أبوك امرأتين إحداهما أمك والثانية أم أخيك، وكانت لأخيك المذكور أخت من الأم بأن كانت أمه متزوجة قبل أبيك بزوج آخر، فولدت منه بنتاً فهي حلال عليك اتفاقاً.
And the sister of his brother by fosterage becomes permissible, just as she becomes permissible by blood lineage: like a brother from the father has a sister from his mother, she becomes permissible for his brother from the father. He said: 'And the sister of his brother by fosterage becomes permissible' – this is an explanation of what is not prohibited by fosterage nor by lineage. He said: 'by fosterage' – it is valid to connect it to the addressee or to the addressee's brother or to both, e.g., you have a blood brother who has a foster sister, so she is halal for you; similarly if you have a foster brother who has a blood sister; and similarly the foster sister of a foster brother. The reason is that what is prohibited by fosterage is only what is prohibited by lineage, and such a kinship by lineage may be non-prohibited, so its like by fosterage is not prohibited. He said: 'like a brother from the father' – its picture: you have a brother from the father, i.e., your father and his father are the same, or your mother is not his mother; that your father married two women, one your mother and the second the mother of your brother; and that mentioned brother has a sister from the mother, i.e., his mother was married before your father to another husband, and she bore a daughter from him, so she is halal for you by agreement.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation. It clarifies that not all foster relationships create marriage prohibitions; only those that correspond to prohibited blood relations. The author gives an example: a man has a half-brother from his father (same father, different mother). That half-brother has a half-sister from his mother (different father). This half-sister is not prohibited in blood (she is the daughter of the stepfather, not a blood relative), so her equivalent in fosterage is also not prohibited. The commentary emphasizes that the prohibition in fosterage is tied to what is prohibited in lineage; since the sister of a half-brother from the father is not a blood relative prohibited in marriage, the sister of a foster brother is likewise permissible. The ruling is unanimous (ittifāqan) among the scholars.

Key Terms
رضاع (radā'): Fosterage relationship established through breastfeeding, creating certain marriage prohibitions.
نسب (nasab): Blood lineage or kinship by birth.
أخ من الأب (akh min al-ab): Half-brother sharing the same father but different mother.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because she corresponds to a half-sister from the mother's side, who is not prohibited in lineage.
C) Because the foster brother is not a mahram.
D) Because the fosterage was not from a complete breastfeeding.

Key Takeaway
The sister of a foster brother is halal because fosterage only prohibits what lineage prohibits; the example shows a blood half-sister from the mother's side is halal, and her foster equivalent is likewise halal.

Two Infants of One Breast are Like Siblings
Explains that two infants who breastfed from same woman become like siblings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the relationship between two infants who breastfed from the same woman and apply it to determine prohibition of marriage.

Core Idea
Two infants of one breast are like siblings — marriage is haram regardless of time or father.

Fosterage (rada') creates family relationships that affect marriage prohibitions. While the general rule is that the wet nurse becomes a mother and her husband a father, this passage clarifies the specific relationship between two infants who shared the same breast. The ruling is absolute: whether they fed at the same time or different times, and whether the husband is the same or different, the two infants become like siblings and marriage between them is forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Recognize that this passage establishes a specific consequence of fosterage: two infants who breastfed from the same woman are treated as siblings for marriage prohibition.
The principle derives from the hadith: 'Fosterage makes unlawful what is unlawful by lineage.'
2
Note the absolute phrasing
The commentator points out that the phrase 'two infants of a breast' is absolute, indicating the ruling applies in all circumstances. The time of breastfeeding and the husband of the wet nurse do not affect the relationship.
This is an expansion of the base text, clarifying that no additional conditions are needed.
3
Understand the degree of relationship
The passage further specifies the type of sibling relationship: full sibling (if milk from one husband), maternal half-sibling (if different husbands), or paternal half-sibling (if same father but different mothers).
This analogy helps in applying further rulings, such as whom else becomes prohibited.
4
Apply to marriage prohibition
The direct consequence is that the two infants cannot marry each other, just as siblings cannot marry. This prohibition is permanent and independent of any other factors.
This is a key ruling in nikah, ensuring that unintended incest is avoided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And two infants of a breast are like a brother and sister. He said: 'and two infants...' – this is a subject, its predicate is his saying 'like a brother and sister.' This issue, even if understood from what passed that fosterage makes the wet nurse a mother and her husband a father, is mentioned separately for clarification and as a prelude to mentioning the issue of partnership in the milk of an animal. The absolute wording of 'two infants' indicates the absoluteness of the ruling: one infant becomes prohibited to the other infant, whether the time of their breastfeeding was the same or different, and whether the husband from whose milk they drank was one or more. He said: 'like a brother and sister' – full siblings if the milk they drank was from one man; or from the mother if not like that; and from the father if a man had two wives and they gave birth to him, then each one breastfed another's child.

Commentary
This passage explains a secondary ruling on fosterage: two infants who breastfed from the same woman become like siblings, even if they are not directly related through the wet nurse as mother. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition applies regardless of whether they fed at the same time or different times, and regardless of whether the husband (whose milk caused the relationship) is the same or different. The relationship can be full siblings (if milk from same husband), maternal (if different fathers), or paternal (if the same father but different mothers). This sets the foundation for the later discussion on partnership in animal milk.

Fosterage Only with Human Milk
States that fosterage prohibitions only apply to human milk, not animal milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that fosterage prohibitions only arise from human milk and distinguish it from animal milk.

Core Idea
Only human milk creates fosterage — animal milk has no legal effect.

In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage (rida') establishes certain marriage prohibitions, but not every form of lactation counts. This lesson clarifies a key limitation: only human milk can create a fosterage relationship. You might wonder whether an infant suckling an animal would create milk kinship, but the school is clear that it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is drinking animal milk (e.g., goat's milk). According to the text, this act is permissible (mubah) and carries no legal consequences related to fosterage.
The ruling here is about the absence of prohibition, not a positive obligation.
2
Identify the legal effect of fosterage
Fosterage normally creates mahram relationships, making marriage between the nursling and certain others prohibited. This effect is exclusive to human milk.
The key is that the prohibition arises from the milk itself, not from the act of suckling per se.
3
Note the decisive factor
The decisive factor is the source of the milk: human milk vs. animal milk. Even if the infant suckles directly from the udder, the milk remains animal milk and does not trigger fosterage rules.
This is a fundamental principle agreed upon by all schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شاريا لبن شاة. قوله : لا شارياً لبن شاة؛ حاصله : أنّ الرضاعة المحرمة إنما هو برضاعة لبن الإنسان لا بلبن الحيوان كالشاة والبقر وإن كان بمص ضرعهما.
Not a buyer of goat's milk. He said: 'not a buyer of goat's milk' – its summary is that the prohibited fosterage is only by breastfeeding human milk, not by the milk of an animal like goat or cow, even if by sucking their udders.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This brief ruling clarifies that the legal effects of fosterage (like marriage prohibitions) are exclusive to human milk. Drinking animal milk, even directly from the udder, does not create any fosterage relationship. This is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh and is agreed upon by the school.

Key Terms
لبن شاة (laban shāh): Goat's milk, used as example of animal milk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, unless the milk is mixed with human milk.

Key Takeaway
Fosterage prohibitions are exclusive to human milk; animal milk has no such effect.

Ruling on Mixed Milk: Dominance and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Explains the rule of dominance when milk is mixed and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of dominance for mixed milk and explain the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding two women's milk.

Core Idea
When milk is mixed, dominance determines prohibition; but between two human milks, the rule is disputed.

In Hanafi fiqh, the prohibition of fosterage through milk depends on the substance consumed. But what if the milk is mixed with other liquids, or with another woman's milk? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the decisive criterion of dominance (ghalabah) and presents an intra-Hanafi difference between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two types of mixtures: (1) human milk mixed with water, medicine, or animal milk; (2) human milk mixed with another woman's milk.
2
Explain the agreed rule for mixing with non-milk
For scenario (1), all Hanafi imams agree: dominance (ghalabah) is considered. If the woman's milk is dominant or equal, prohibition stands; if overcome, not.
The overcome milk is considered mustahlik (consumed).
3
State Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf (and one narration from Abu Hanifa) applies the same dominance rule to scenario (2): only the woman whose milk is dominant becomes a foster mother.
This is the view chosen in many matn texts like al-Kanz, al-Multaqa.
4
State Muhammad's view
Muhammad (and another narration from Abu Hanifa) holds that prohibition attaches to both women absolutely, whether equal or one dominant.
His reasoning: the same genus does not dominate the same genus.
5
Note the tarjih and its rationale
Al-Hidayah prefers Muhammad's view, considering it al-azhar (most apparent) and al-ahwat (most precautionary). The reasoning is that genus does not overcome genus.
This preference is also echoed in al-Ghayah and Sharh al-Majma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم خلط لبنها بماء أو دواء أو لبن امرأة أخرى أو شاة بالغلبة. قوله : وحكـم ... الخ؛ يعني إذا خلط لبن امرأة بماء أو دواء أو لبن شاة وغيرها، تعتبر الغلبة؛ فإن كان لبن المرأةِ غالباً على غيره يثبت به التحريم وإن كان مغلوبا لا؛ لأن المغلوب كالمستهلك، وإن استويا يثبتُ التحريم أيضاً، وهذا كله إجماعي بين أئمتنا .. وإن اختلط لبن امرأة بلبن امرأة أخرى فشربه فعند أبي يوسف له تعتبر فيه أيضاً الغلبة، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة الله، فمن يكون لبنه غالباً يتعلق به التحريم فقط دون الأخرى، وعند محمد الله وهو رواية عنه يتعلق التحريم بكليهما مطلقاً، استوى لبنهما أو غلب أحدهما على الآخر. وإلى ترجيحه مال صاحب «الهداية» (۲) ، وعلله بأنّ الجنس لا يغلب الجنس، وفي «شرح المجمع : قيل : إنّه الأصح، وفي «الغاية»: هو الأظهر والأحوط، ومن هاهنا يظهرُ ما في قول المصنف من المسامحة بوجهين. (۱) هذا على قول أبي يوسف الله، وهو اختيار المتون مثل: «الكنز» (٥۰)، و«الملتقى»(ص٥٧)، والنقاية (ص) (۸۳) ، وغيرها، وعند محمد الله تتعلق الحرمة بهما وعن الإمام روايتان، ورجح بعض المشايخ قول محمد وفي (الغاية) هو أظهر وأحوط ، وقيل : إنه أصح، وهو اختيار صاحب الهداية» (۱) : ۲۲٥)؛ لتأخّر دليل محمد الله. ينظر : ((الشرنبلالية»(١ : ٣٥٧).
And the ruling on mixing her milk with water, or medicine, or the milk of another woman, or a goat, according to dominance. He said: 'and the ruling...' – meaning: if a woman's milk is mixed with water, medicine, goat's milk, etc., dominance is considered; if the woman's milk is dominant over others, prohibition is established, and if it is overcome, not, because the overcome is like consumed; if they are equal, prohibition is also established. All this is consensus among our imams. And if the milk of one woman is mixed with the milk of another woman and then drunk, according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) dominance is also considered therein, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa; whichever milk is dominant, prohibition attaches to it alone, not the other. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), and it is a narration from him, prohibition attaches to both absolutely, whether their milk is equal or one dominates the other. The author of al-Hidayah leaned towards preferring his view, reasoning that the same genus does not overcome the same genus. In Sharh al-Majma' it is said that it is the most correct; in al-Ghayah it is the most apparent and most precautionary. From here appears what is in the author's statement of looseness in two respects. (1) This is according to Abu Yusuf, and it is the choice of texts like al-Kanz, al-Multaqa, al-Niqayah, etc. According to Muhammad, prohibition attaches to both; from Imam there are two narrations. Some mashayikh preferred Muhammad's view; in al-Ghayah it is the most apparent and most precautionary; and it is said it is the most correct, and it is the choice of the author of al-Hidayah because Muhammad's proof is later. See al-Shurunbulaliyyah.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage covers two main scenarios: (1) mixing human milk with water, medicine, or animal milk: the rule is dominance (ghalabah). If human milk is dominant, prohibition is established; if not, no prohibition. If equal, prohibition is also established. This is agreed upon. (2) mixing human milk of two women: there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Yusuf (and one narration from Abu Hanifa) applies dominance: only the dominant woman's milk creates prohibition. Muhammad (and another narration from Abu Hanifa) says both women's milk creates prohibition regardless of dominance. The author of al-Hidayah preferred Muhammad's view (since genus does not dominate genus), and later texts like al-Ghayah consider it more apparent and precautionary. The footnote adds that many matn texts choose Abu Yusuf's view, but some mashayikh and al-Hidayah prefer Muhammad's. Thus there are two positions: one choosing dominance, the other absolute prohibition for both.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Dominance or preponderance; used to determine when mixed milk still creates prohibition.
مستهلك (mustahlik): Consumed or overcome; something that loses its identity in a mixture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the woman whose milk is dominant becomes a foster mother.
B) Both women become foster mothers regardless of proportions.
C) Neither becomes a foster mother if the mixture is equal.
D) The prohibition depends on the amount consumed.

Key Takeaway
When two human milks mix, the dominant view in the Hanafi school (preferred by al-Hidayah) establishes fosterage from both women, while Abu Yusuf's view considers only the dominant one.

Milk Mixed with Food: Absolute Permissibility
States ruling with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a woman's milk is mixed with food and explain the reasoning based on the concepts of asl (original) and tabi' (subordinate).

Core Idea
Milk mixed with food is always halal: food is the base (asl), milk is subordinate, so no fosterage prohibition arises.

In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage (rida') prohibits marriage when milk is consumed directly. But what if milk is mixed with solid food? This lesson examines a clear ruling from Abu Hanifa: the mixture is always permissible, regardless of the proportion of milk. The underlying principle is that food is the original substance (asl) and milk is subordinate (tabi'); therefore, the mixture is treated as if the milk is overcome (maghlub) by the food.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is eating food mixed with a woman's milk. The ruling is absolute permissibility (halal). No fosterage prohibition is established from this mixture.
This applies whether the milk is physically dominant, equal, or less.
2
Understand the Reasoning: Asl and Tabi'
The food is considered the original substance (asl) because it is the primary component in terms of purpose. The milk is subordinate (tabi'). When a subordinate is mixed with an original, the subordinate is considered overcome (maghlub) by ruling, even if it is physically dominant.
Hence the mixture takes the ruling of the food (halal), not the milk (which could otherwise cause fosterage if consumed alone).
3
Recognize the Scope: Absolute
The ruling is 'mutaqan' (absolutely) applicable regardless of dominance. This means no further investigation into proportions is needed.
This is a convenient rule to avoid uncertainty.
4
Note the Authority: Abu Hanifa
The passage attributes this ruling to Abu Hanifa. Other Hanafi scholars (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) may have differed, but this is the position from the Zahir al-Riwayah.
In practice, fatwa often relies on this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطعام الحل. قوله : وبطعام؛ يعني إذا اختلط لبنُ امرأة بطعام فأكله فحكمه الحلّ مطلقاً، غلب اللبن أو لا؛ لأن الطعام أصل، واللبنُ تابع في حق المقصود، فصار كالمغلوب حكماً، وإن كان مستوياً أو غالباً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة الله.
And with food, it is halal. He said: 'and with food' – meaning: if a woman's milk is mixed with food and he eats it, its ruling is absolute permissibility, whether the milk dominates or not, because food is the original (asl) and milk is subordinate in terms of purpose, so it becomes like the overcome by ruling, even if it is equal or dominant. This is according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling when human milk is mixed with solid food: the mixture is always halal, whether the milk is dominant, equal, or less. The reasoning is that food is the original substance (asl), and the milk is subordinate (tabi') in the intended purpose of eating. Thus, the milk is considered overcome (maghlub) by ruling, even if physically it is dominant. This is the view of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The base or original substance; here, food is considered the asl.
تابع (tābi'): Subordinate or secondary; here, milk is subordinate to food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when milk is mixed with food, the ruling is:
A) Permissible only if the food is dominant.
B) Permissible absolutely, regardless of dominance.
C) Forbidden if the milk is dominant.
D) Permissible only if the milk is less than the food.

Key Takeaway
Milk mixed with food is always halal because food is the original substance and milk is subordinate, so no fosterage prohibition arises.

The Principle of Ghalebah in Mixing Milk for Fosterage
Explaining the rule of preponderance when milk is mixed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of ghalebah in milk mixtures for fosterage and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding mixtures of two human milks.

Core Idea
When human milk is mixed, its preponderance determines fosterage; but with another human milk, the mufta-bihi prohibits from both.

In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage (rida') creates marriage prohibitions only with human milk. But what if the milk a child drinks is mixed with water, medicine, or another woman's milk? This lesson unpacks the principle of ghalebah (preponderance) that the Hanafi school applies to resolve such mixtures, and a key internal dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding mixtures of two human milks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational rule
First, confirm that only human milk creates fosterage prohibition. Animal milk, such as sheep or goat, does not establish kinship bonds.
This is the basis for all subsequent rulings on mixtures.
2
Apply ghalebah to mixtures with non-milk substances
When human milk is mixed with water, medicine, or animal milk, the ruling depends on preponderance: if human milk is dominant (ghālib) or equal, prohibition is established; if subordinate (maghlūb), no prohibition. This is consensus among the Hanafi imams.
The subordinate is considered consumed (al-maghlūb ka al-mustahlik).
3
Contrast the intra-Hanafi dispute on two human milks
Abu Yusuf applies the same ghalebah principle to mixtures of two human milks: only the dominant woman's milk prohibits. Muhammad holds that both milks prohibit absolutely, reasoning that one genus cannot overpower another.
Both views are transmitted from Abu Hanifa.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Hidayah and later authorities prefer Muhammad's view as more precautionary. Thus the relied-upon position is that when a child drinks milk from two women, both become foster mothers, widening the circle of prohibition.
The rationale: 'al-jins lā yaghlib al-jins' (one genus does not overcome another).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شاريا لبن شاة، وحكم خلط لبنها بماء أو دواء أو لبن امرأة أخرى أو شاة بالغلبة. حاصله: أن الرضاعة المحرمة إنما هو برضاعة لبن الإنسان لا بلبن الحيوان. وإذا خلط لبن امرأة بماء أو دواء أو لبن شاة وغيرها، تعتبر الغلبة؛ فإن كان لبن المرأة غالباً على غيره يثبت به التحريم وإن كان مغلوباً لا؛ لأن المغلوب كالمستهلك، وإن استويا يثبت التحريم أيضاً، وهذا كله إجماعي بين أئمتنا. وإن اختلط لبن امرأة بلبن امرأة أخرى فشربه فعند أبي يوسف تعتبر فيه الغلبة أيضاً، وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة، فمن يكون لبنه غالباً يتعلق به التحريم فقط دون الأخرى، وعند محمد يتعلق التحريم بكليهما مطلقاً، استوى لبنهما أو غلب أحدهما على الآخر. وإلى ترجيح قول محمد مال صاحب الهداية وعلله بأن الجنس لا يغلب الجنس.
Not buying a sheep's milk, and the ruling of mixing her milk with water, or medicine, or another woman's milk, or a sheep's milk, based on preponderance. Summary: Prohibited fosterage is only by suckling human milk, not animal milk. When a woman's milk is mixed with water, medicine, sheep's milk, etc., preponderance is considered: if the woman's milk is dominant, prohibition is established; if subordinate, no; because the subordinate is like consumed; and if equal, prohibition is also established. All of this is consensus among our imams. If a woman's milk is mixed with another woman's milk and [a child] drinks it, then according to Abu Yusuf preponderance is also considered (a transmission from Abu Hanifa): only the milk of the dominant woman establishes prohibition, not the other. According to Muhammad (another transmission from Abu Hanifa), prohibition is established from both absolutely, whether their milks are equal or one dominates. The author of al-Hidayah inclined to prefer Muhammad's view, reasoning that one genus does not overpower another.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This is a commentary (sharh) on a matn that states rulings on fosterage when human milk is mixed. The commentator begins by clarifying that only human milk, not animal milk, creates fosterage prohibition. He then explains the principle of ghalebah (preponderance) when human milk is mixed with other substances: water, medicine, or animal milk. The ruling is a consensus among the Hanafi imams: if the human milk is dominant or equal to the mixture, fosterage is established; if it is subordinate, no prohibition arises. Then he discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mixing milk of two women. Abu Yusuf applies ghalebah: only the dominant woman's milk creates prohibition. Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds that prohibition is established from both women, regardless of proportion. The commentator notes that the author of al-Hidayah preferred Muhammad's view, arguing that one genus (milk) does not overcome another genus. Later Hanafi works like Sharh al-Majma' and al-Ghayah regard Muhammad's view as the stronger and more precautionary position.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalebah): Preponderance; the criterion used to determine which component of a mixture is dominant and thus confers its ruling. If human milk is ghālib (dominant) in a mixture with other substances, fosterage prohibition applies.
الرضاع (al-ridāʿ): Fosterage; the relationship established by a child suckling human milk under specific conditions, which creates marriage prohibitions equivalent to those of blood kinship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the woman whose milk is dominant becomes a foster mother.
B) Both women become foster mothers, widening the circle of prohibition.
C) No fosterage is established because the milk is mixed.
D) The child must choose which woman to consider as foster mother.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses preponderance (ghalebah) to determine fosterage in mixed milk, and the relied-upon view for two human milks is that both women become foster mothers.

Rulings on Mixing Milk with Food and Special Cases
Further fosterage rulings on exceptional cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on four exceptional fosterage cases: milk mixed with food, man's milk, enema, and milk from a bikr or deceased woman.

Core Idea
Food dominates milk in mixtures; man's milk is not real milk; enema is not drinking; bikr/deceased milk creates fosterage.

Fosterage (rida') normally requires drinking human milk in the prescribed quantity. However, several borderline cases arise: when milk is mixed with food, when milk comes from a man, when milk enters the body through an enema, or when the woman is never married or deceased. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies each ruling according to Abu Hanifa and the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four exceptional cases
List the four scenarios: (1) milk mixed with food, (2) man's milk, (3) milk given via enema, (4) milk from an unmarried (bikr) or deceased woman.
These are all cases where the application of normal fosterage rules is ambiguous.
2
State the ruling for each case
For each, state whether fosterage prohibition (haram) is established or not. According to Abu Hanifa: (1) no prohibition, (2) no prohibition, (3) no prohibition, (4) prohibition established.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each ruling
For (1): Food is the original (asl), milk is subordinate (tābiʿ) in purpose. For (2): Man's milk is not real milk. For (3): Fosterage requires drinking, not just reaching the interior. For (4): Absolute texts cover all women regardless of marital status or life status.
The reasoning relies on Hanafi principles of purpose (maqsud) and literal reading.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on food mixture
Abu Hanifa's view is that food always dominates; Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) apply the concept of ghalebah (dominance) to determine the ruling. This ikhtilaf is mentioned in the text.
The passage attributes the ruling explicitly to Abu Hanifa, implying it is the relied-upon view.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a child who drinks milk from a woman who passed away while lactating. The ruling from this passage is that fosterage is established because the absolute texts include deceased women.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وبطعام؛ يعني إذا اختلط لبن امرأة بطعام فأكله فحكمه الحلّ مطلقاً، غلب اللبن أو لا؛ لأن الطعام أصل، واللبن تابع في حق المقصود، فصار كالمغلوب حكماً، وإن كان مستوياً أو غالباً، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وأما لبن الرجل فإذا نزل للرجل لبن فشربه صبي لا يتعلق به حرمة الرضاع، لأنه ليس بلبن حقيقة، بل هو رطوبة شبيهة به، كدم السمك ليس بدم حقيقة. واحتقان صبي بلبنها لا يحرم، لأن أحكام الرضاعة تثبت بشرب اللبن لا بمجرد الوصول إلى الباطن. وتحرم الرضاعة بلبن البكر (التي لم تجامع قط) ولبن الميتة، لأن نصوص حرمة الرضاع مطلقة شاملة.
His statement: 'And with food' – meaning if a woman's milk is mixed with food and [a child] eats it, its ruling is absolutely permissible (halal), whether the milk dominates or not, because food is the original and milk is subordinate in terms of purpose, so it becomes like the overridden legally, even if equal or dominant. This is according to Abu Hanifa. As for a man's milk, if a man has milk and a child drinks it, no fosterage prohibition attaches to it, because it is not milk in reality, but moisture resembling it, like fish blood not being blood in reality. And giving an enema of her milk to a child does not make it forbidden, because the rules of fosterage are established by drinking milk, not merely by its reaching the interior. Fosterage is forbidden (i.e., established) with the milk of an unmarried woman (who has never had intercourse) and with the milk of a deceased woman, because the texts on the prohibition of fosterage are absolute and inclusive.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn that continues the rulings on fosterage. It covers four distinct points: 1) When human milk is mixed with food and eaten, it never creates fosterage prohibition according to Abu Hanifa, because food is the 'asl (original) and milk is subordinate (tābi'). This is because the purpose of eating food is nourishment, not the milk. Even if the milk is dominant or equal, the mixture is considered like a subordinate. 2) A man's milk does not create fosterage; it is considered only a moisture resembling milk, like fish blood, which is not real blood. 3) Administering milk to a child through an enema does not establish fosterage because fosterage requires actual drinking (shurb) of milk; merely reaching the interior through a different means is insufficient. 4) Milk from a woman who has never had intercourse (bikr) and from a deceased woman does create fosterage, because the revealed texts on fosterage are absolute and include all women, regardless of marital status or life status.

Key Terms
التابع (al-tābiʿ): Subordinate; in the context of mixing milk with food, the milk is considered subordinate to the food because the purpose (maqsud) is eating food, so the mixture takes the ruling of the dominant element (food) and no fosterage is established.
الحقنة (al-ḥuqnah): Enema; administering liquid through the rectum. The text states that if a child is given an enema of a woman's milk, it does not create fosterage because fosterage requires drinking (shurb), not mere entry into the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fosterage prohibition is established because the milk is still human milk.
B) No fosterage prohibition because the food is the original and the milk is subordinate.
C) Fosterage is established only if the milk is dominant over the food.
D) The ruling depends on the amount of milk present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fosterage is established only by drinking pure human milk; mixtures, man's milk, and enemas do not qualify, while milk from a bikr or deceased woman does.

Definition and Ruling of Khul' in Hanafi Fiqh
Introduces khul' chapter with definition and condition of need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khul', identify the condition of need that makes it permissible, and explain the Quranic evidence and its practical implication.

Core Idea
Khul' is a compensated divorce permissible only when genuine discord makes reconciliation impossible.

This lesson introduces the chapter on khul' from a Hanafi commentary. You will learn the precise legal definition, the condition that triggers its permissibility (hajah), and the primary Quranic evidence. Understanding the condition of need is crucial to avoid the sin of seeking divorce without cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal definition
Extract the definition of khul' from the commentary: it is the removal of the marriage bond contingent on the wife's acceptance, using the word khul' or its equivalent, with compensation. Distinguish it from regular divorce (talaq) which does not require acceptance.
The key distinction is that khul' requires the wife's acceptance and payment of compensation.
2
Understand the condition for permissibility (need)
The commentary states that khul' is permissible 'when need occurs', defined as the occurrence of disagreement such that harmony cannot be hoped for. This is derived from the Quranic phrase 'if you fear that they cannot uphold the limits of Allah'. Without need, it is not permissible.
Need (hajah) is a legal condition (shart) for permissibility, not just a recommendation.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence (dalil)
The primary evidence is Quran 2:229, which permits the wife to ransom herself when both spouses fear not upholding Allah's limits. The phrase 'fima iftadat bih' (in what she ransoms herself with) establishes the basis for compensation.
The dalil is qati' al-thubut (certain transmission) but zanni al-dalala (open to interpretation) - the condition of fear is key.
4
Note the ruling when need is absent
When there is no genuine need (e.g., mere dislike without serious discord), khul' is not permissible. The commentator emphasizes that even when needed, it is better to avoid it if possible, due to the prophetic disapproval of divorce.
The hadith that divorce is the most detested permissible thing applies here.
5
Recognize the related hadiths
The commentary cites hadiths warning against seeking khul' without cause: 'The women who seek khul' are the hypocrites' and 'Any woman who asks for divorce without a cause, Paradise is forbidden for her.' These reinforce the need for genuine hardship.
These hadiths are from al-Tirmidhi and are classified as hasan.

Source Text

Chapter of Khul'. [1] His statement: Chapter of Khul'; it was delayed after Ila while the appropriate order would have been to place it before it, because it is divorce unlike Ila. Because Ila can be divorce without compensation, while Khul is divorce with compensation, so Ila is closer to divorce. Also, Ila is transgression and ill-conduct from the husband, and Khul is ill-conduct from the wife, so Khul is more deserving of being later. As in al-‘Inayah. Khul (with fatha) means removal; it is said: He removed his garment from his body; i.e. took it off. With damm it is a noun; it is said: The woman performed khul': when she ransomed herself from him with her wealth. As in al-Kifayah. They defined it legally as per al-Fath (1) and others: that it is the removal of the ownership of marriage, contingent upon her acceptance, with the word khul' or its equivalent. So if he says: I divorce you, and intends divorce, it occurs as one irrevocable divorce, and it is not khul' because it is not contingent upon her acceptance. And if he divorces her for a consideration, it is not khul' nor does it cancel the dower. The basis for it is His saying: {Divorce is twice; then retention in honor or release with kindness. And it is not lawful for you to take anything from what you have given them unless both fear that they cannot uphold the limits of Allah. So if you fear that they cannot uphold the limits of Allah, there is no sin on them in what she ransoms herself with.} [2] His statement: There is no harm in it; i.e. in khul' when need occurs, which is the occurrence of disagreement between the spouses such that harmony and good companionship cannot be hoped for. He indicated by it that when there is no need, it is not permissible, and even when there is need, it is better to avoid it as much as possible. (1) Fath al-Qadir (4:210-211). (2) Al-Baqara: part of verse 229. (3) Regarding the realization of need, there is the statement of our master Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'When women desire khul', do not prevent them' in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (4:184), and this meaning is affirmed by his statement: 'Do not force your young women to marry a despicable man, for they love from that what you love' in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq (4:184). This is supported by the hadith that divorce is the most detestable of permissible things as mentioned earlier, and the hadith: 'The women who seek khul' are the hypocrites' narrated by al-Tirmidhi, and the hadith: 'Any woman who asks her husband for divorce without a cause, the fragrance of Paradise is forbidden for her' narrated by al-Tirmidhi.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a commentary introducing the chapter on khul' (divorce at the wife's initiative by paying compensation). The commentator first explains why the chapter on khul' is placed after the chapter on ila (vow of abstention from intercourse): although khul' is a form of divorce, ila is closer to pure divorce because it can result in divorce without compensation, while khul' is divorce with compensation. Additionally, ila involves transgression by the husband, while khul' involves transgression by the wife, so khul' is more appropriately placed later. The commentator then provides the linguistic and legal definition of khul'. Linguistically, khul' (with fatha) means 'removal', and as a noun (with damm) refers to a woman ransoming herself. Legally, it is defined as the termination of the marriage bond contingent upon the wife's acceptance, using the word khul' or its equivalent. If the husband says 'I divorce you' intending divorce, it becomes a single irrevocable divorce and is not khul' because it does not require her acceptance. Similarly, if he divorces her for a consideration, it is not khul' and does not cancel the dower. The primary evidence is Quran 2:229, which permits the wife to ransom herself when both fear they cannot uphold Allah's limits. The commentator then discusses the condition of need (hajah): khul' is permissible when there is genuine marital discord and reconciliation is unlikely. When there is no need, it is not permissible, and even when needed, it is better to avoid it. This is supported by statements of Umar ibn al-Khattab and hadiths showing the severity of divorce and seeking khul' without cause.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): Legal: removal of marriage ownership contingent on wife's acceptance with word khul' or its equivalent, with compensation.
نشوز (nushuz): Disobedience or ill-conduct by either spouse leading to marital discord.
حاجة (hajah): Need; in khul' context, the condition of severe marital discord making reconciliation impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is khul' permissible?
A) At any time the wife desires it
B) Only when there is genuine marital discord and fear of not upholding Allah's limits
C) Only when the husband agrees without compensation
D) Only after one divorce has already occurred

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a compensated, need-based divorce in Hanafi fiqh: permissible only when genuine discord threatens the marital limits of Allah, and even then, it is better to avoid it if possible.

Condition of Valid Compensation and Nature of Khul' as Bain Divorce
States condition of valid compensation and dispute on khul' being divorce or dissolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid compensation in khul' and explain why the Hanafi school considers khul' a bain divorce rather than a dissolution.

Core Idea
Khul' compensation must be valid property like dower, and the resulting divorce is irrevocable (bain), not a dissolution.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies two essential points about khul': what constitutes valid consideration, and the legal nature of the resulting separation. Understanding these is crucial for distinguishing khul' from other forms of marital dissolution and for ensuring its validity according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage addresses two separate but related issues: what constitutes valid compensation in khul', and the legal nature of khul' (divorce vs. dissolution). Recognize that both are governed by the rules of mahr (dower).
2
Analyze the condition of compensation
The compensation in khul' must be property that can validly serve as mahr. This means it must be a valuable, legally recognized property (mal muttaqawwim). Non-property (e.g., blood, carrion) or non-fungible wealth (e.g., a service that is not valuable) is invalid.
The condition mirrors that of mahr in marriage; see the Hanafi rules on mahr.
3
State the Hanafi position on the nature of khul'
The Hanafi school holds that khul' is a bain (irrevocable) divorce, counting as one of the three allowed divorces. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
Hanafi: all three imams agree; it is not a dissolution (faskh).
4
Identify the opposing view
Some early scholars, including Ibn Abbas and Imam al-Shafi'i in his old opinion, considered khul' a dissolution (faskh) rather than a divorce. This means it would not count as a divorce and would merely annul the marriage.
Shafi'i later retracted to the view that it is a divorce.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi proof and response
The Hanafi proof relies on Companion narrations: from Uthman (recorded by Malik) and from Ali who said 'no irrevocable divorce except in ransom or ila'. The opposing argument uses the Quranic term 'fatada' (ransom) which suggests dissolution. The Hanafi response is that the weight of Companion consensus supports divorce, and the Shafi'i school itself eventually agreed.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the number of available divorces is reduced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١]قوله : بما يصلح مهراً ؛ أي بمال يصح جعله مهراً، فلا يصح بما ليس بمال ،
وبما هو مال غير متقوم. [۲] قوله : طلاق بائن ؛ اختلف فيه، فقيل : إنّه فسخ وفرقةٌ من غير طلاق، وهو المروي عن ابن عباس الله ، أخرجه أحمد، وبه قال الشافعي في القديم، لكن الصحيح من مذهبه المرجوع إليه أنه طلاق، كما صرح به التفتازاني في «التلويح»، وهو المروي عن عثمان ، أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ»، وقال علي الله : «لا تكون طلقة بائنة إلا في
فدية أو إيلاء» (۳) ، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة، ومثله أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة عن ابن مسعود
(۱) في «مصنف عبد الرزاق (٤) : ۱۸۳) ، و(سنن الترمذي (۳): (٤۹۲) ، وقال : حديث غريب وليس إسناده بالقوي، وسنن البيهقي الكبير (۷) : (٣١٦) ، و(مسند الربيع»(١ : ٣٦٢)، و مسند أبي يعلى(۱۱ : ۱۱۰) ، و(المعجم الكبير (۱۷) : (۳۳۹ ، وقال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٥ : ) رواه الطبراني، وفيه قيس بن الربيع وثقة الثوري وشعبة، وفيه ضعف وبقية رجاله رجال الصحيح. (۲) في (مصنف عبد الرزاق (٤) :) (۱۸۳) ، و«المنتقی (۱): (۱۸۷) ، و«المستدرك (۲ : ۲۱۸)، وقال الحاكم : حديث صحيح على شرط الشيخين ولم يخرجاه، و سنن الدارمي (٢ : ٢١٦)، و سنن سعيد بن منصور (۱) : (۳۷۳) و «سنن ابن ماجة (١) : (٦٦٢)، و(مصنف ابن أبي شيبة»(٤ : ١٩٥)، و(المعجم الأوسط (٥) : (۳۳۳) ، و(مسند أحمد»(۵ : ۲۲۷)، و(مسند الروياني»(۱) : ٤١١) ، وشعب الإيمان (٤) : (۳۹۰)، و«الفردوس (١ : ٣٥١)، وغيرها. وعن أبي موسى له قال : «إن الله تعالى لا يحب الذواقين والذواقات» في «مسند البزار (۸: ۷۰، ۷۱)، و(المعجم الأوسط (۸) : (٢٤) ، قال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٤ : ٣٣٥) : (وأحد أسانيد البزار فيه عمران القطان، وثقه أحمد وابن حبان، وضعفه يحيى بن سعيد وغيره، وعن عبادة بن الصامت لهلهلهلهلهله عند الطبراني، راو لم يسم وبقية إسناده حسن». (۳) ينظر: «تلخيص الحبير» (٣: ٢٠٥).
[1] His statement: 'With what is valid as dower'; meaning with wealth that is valid to be made dower. So it is not valid with something that is not wealth (non-property) nor with something that is non-fungible wealth. [2] His statement: 'It is an irrevocable divorce.' There is disagreement about it. Some said it is a dissolution and separation without divorce. This is narrated from Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, recorded by Ahmad. And this is the old view of al-Shafi'i, but the correct view of his school that he retracted to is that it is divorce, as al-Taftazani clarified in al-Talwih. It is narrated from Uthman, recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta. And Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'There is no irrevocable divorce except in ransom or ila.' Recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba. And similar was recorded by Ibn Abi Shayba from Ibn Mas'ud. (Footnotes on hadith sources omitted in translation)
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the conditions of khul'. First, it specifies that the compensation (consideration) in khul' must be something that can validly serve as dower (mahr). This means it must be property of value that is fungible (muttaqawwim). It cannot be something that is not considered property (e.g., a non-valuable item) or something that is property but not legally fungible (e.g., blood or carrion). Next, the passage addresses the legal nature of khul': it states that khul' results in an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain). However, there is a dispute: some scholars (including the Companion Ibn Abbas and Imam al-Shafi'i in his earlier opinion) hold that khul' is a dissolution (faskh) of the marriage, not a divorce. This means it does not count as one of the three divorces and the marriage is simply annulled. But the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school (and the later Shafi'i view) is that khul' is indeed a divorce, specifically an irrevocable (bain) divorce. The passage cites narrations from Uthman and Ali supporting the bain divorce view. The Hanafi school adopts this view. The passage concludes with extensive hadith references (though garbled in OCR) supporting the dislike of seeking divorce without cause.

Key Terms
بائن (bā’in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot remarry her without a new contract and her consent.
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution of marriage without counting as divorce; retroactive annulment.
مهر (mahr): Dower; mandatory payment from husband to wife in marriage, must be property of value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of khul'?
A) A dissolution (faskh) of the marriage
B) A form of irrevocable divorce (talaq bain)
C) A revocable divorce (talaq raj'i)
D) An annulment retroactive from the beginning

Key Takeaway
Khul' is a valid bain divorce when the compensation is property that could serve as dower; it is not a dissolution.
