Conditions for Ordering Maintenance from an Absent Person's Property
Conditions for ordering maintenance from absent person's property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for ordering maintenance from an absent person's property and identify which relatives qualify.

Core Idea
Maintenance from an absent's property is only for wife, child, and parents, and only from property of the same type as their right.

When a husband or father is absent and has left property with a depositary, debtor, or business partner, can a judge order maintenance for his family? The Hanafi school sets precise conditions to protect both the absent person's rights and the family's needs. This passage explains which relatives qualify, what property can be used, and what procedural safeguards are required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the judge's order for maintenance from an absent person's property is the act under discussion. The ruling (hukm) is that it is permissible and wajib under certain conditions.
The judge's order is an assistance (iʿānah) for wife, child, and parents, but a new obligation for other relatives.
2
Analyze the condition regarding property type
The maintenance must be from property of the same type (jins) as the right claimed—food, clothing, dirhams, dinars. Trade goods requiring sale are excluded because selling the absent's property is not allowed.
This prevents unjustified disposal of assets that might be needed later.
3
Identify the limited category of relatives
Only the wife, child (free poor), and parents (poor and needy) qualify. Other relatives, like siblings, are not included because their maintenance is not obligatory before a judicial order. The judge's order would create a new obligation, which is not permitted against an absent person.
The key distinction is whether the relative had a right to take property before a judicial order.
4
Understand the procedural requirements
The holder of the property (depositary, debtor, muḍārib) must acknowledge both the property and the family relationship, or the judge must have personal knowledge. The woman must take an oath that she has not received maintenance, and she must provide a surety to protect the absent person's property.
Takfīl (surety) and taḥlīf (oath) prevent fraud and safeguard the absent person's rights.

(When deposited) And in restricting [the judge's] ordering of maintenance for the wife, parents, and child, there is an indication that he does not order from the property of the absent person maintenance for others, such as his brother and every prohibited relative (mahram) except the relationship of parenthood. The reason for that is that their maintenance is not obligatory before a judicial order; therefore they may not take anything from his property if they get hold of it. So the judicial order in their case is an initial imposition, and that is not permissible regarding the absent person because it is not permissible to pass judgment against an absent person in our school, as will come in its place. As opposed to the wife and those related by parenthood, for they may take before a judicial order without his consent, so the order [for them] is assistance, not a judgment. Likewise, maintenance is not ordered for a slave in his absence. Thus in al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurrar. [1] His saying: 'and his child': i.e., the free poor child, and similarly the ruling applies to an adult who is chronically disabled, and a poor female absolutely.
[2] His saying: 'and his parents': i.e., the two poor parents in need of him, as will be mentioned.
[3] His saying: 'of the same type as their right'; the result: their right is only in food, clothing, dirhams, and dinars, so their maintenance is ordered from such property. If he has no property of the same type as their right, but rather trade goods and merchandise needing sale, their maintenance is not ordered from it because selling the property of an absent person is not permissible.

[1] His saying: 'if he acknowledges it...'; the result: maintenance for the mentioned persons is only ordered from the property of the absent person if the one who has his property, such as the depositary, muḍārib, and debtor, acknowledges that the property is with him, and that she is his wife, or that they are his parents, or that he is his child. If he denies any of that, then no order. Similarly, if the judge knows that, i.e., the property and the marriage and the lineage. And if he knows one of them, the acknowledgment of the depositary and others for the other is needed. If the judge does not know either of them and the one who has his property does not acknowledge both or one of them, then no order because it is not possible to prove his property or his relationship by presenting evidence and oath due to the absence of an opponent. Thus in al-Baḥr and others. (4) [2] His saying: 'and he makes her provide a surety' (yukfiluhā) from takfīl, meaning taking a surety, like taḥlīf meaning taking an oath, and taḥlīf is prior to takfīl, even though the author mentioned it later. The result: if the absent person has property with a depositary and others and he acknowledges the marriage and that his property is with him, or the judge knows that, and the woman requests that the judge order her maintenance from that property, the judge makes her swear that her absent husband did not give her the maintenance. Because it is possible that her husband gave her at the time of his travel maintenance for many months in advance. So she swears to show her entitlement. When she swears, the judge determines maintenance for her from that property, and orders the depositary and others to pay that amount to her every month, or every day, or every year according to what the judge sees fit, and he takes a surety from her for the property, as a consideration for the absent person, because perhaps she has taken her maintenance [already] or her husband divorced her and the ʿidda has expired. (1) i.e., each of the depositary, muḍārib, and debtor regarding the property of deposit, muḍāraba, or debt, and regarding the marriage for maintenance of the wife, and regarding lineage for the others. See Majmaʿ al-Anhur 1:494. (2) i.e., the deposit, muḍāraba, debt, marriage, and lineage; because his knowledge is proof, it is permissible to base a judgment on it in his jurisdiction. If he knows some of the three, their acknowledgment is required for what he does not know, which is the correct view. See al-Durr al-Muntaqā 1:494. (3) al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq 4:213.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the ruling for ordering maintenance (nafaqah) for the wife, minor child, and parents of an absent person (ghāʾib) from property left by him with a depositary, debtor, or business partner (muḍārib). The commentator explains the conditions and procedure.

The ruling states that the judge can only order maintenance from the type of property (jins) that corresponds to the right being claimed—namely, dirhams, dinars, food, or clothing. Trade goods (ʿurūḍ) that would need to be sold are not included because selling an absent person's property without his permission is not allowed in the Hanafi school.

The commentator points out that the restriction to wife, child, and parents indicates that maintenance is not ordered for other relatives like a brother or any mahram (prohibited relative) except through direct lineage (qarāba al-wilād). The reason for this distinction is that the maintenance of those other relatives is not obligatory before a judicial order (qaḍāʾ); they have no right to take from his property even if they get hold of it. For such relatives, a judge's order would be an initial creation of obligation (ibtidāʾ ījāb), which is not allowed against an absent person. In contrast, the wife and those related by birth (meaning child and parents) have a right to take maintenance without a judicial order, so the judge's role is merely to assist them (iʿānah) rather than to create a new obligation. Similarly, maintenance is not ordered for a slave in absence.

The condition of the property being with a depositary (mūdaʿ), debtor (madyūn), or muḍārib is specified. The judge's order is conditional on either the acknowledgment (iqrār) of the depositary/debtor/muḍārib regarding both the existence of the property and the family relationship (marriage or lineage), or the judge's own knowledge (ʿilm) of these facts. If both are unknown, no order can be made because proof through witnesses and oath is not possible without an opponent (khaṣm).

Finally, the procedure is outlined: the woman must first swear an oath (taḥlīf) that her husband did not provide her with maintenance, to prevent fraud. Then the judge determines the amount and orders payment. A surety (kafīl) is taken from her to protect the absent person's property in case she later turns out to be unentitled (e.g., if she already received maintenance or was divorced and her ʿidda ended).

Key Terms
مودع (mūdaʿ): A person with whom the absent person deposited his property for safekeeping.
مضارب (muḍārib): A person who receives money from the absent person for a business partnership (muḍārabah) where profit is shared.
مديون (madyūn): A debtor of the absent person, i.e., someone who owes money to the absent person.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession by the holder of the property regarding the existence of the property and the family relationship.
تكفيل (takfīl): The act of taking a surety (kafīl) from the woman to guarantee the return of maintenance money if she turns out to be unentitled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what condition must be met regarding the type of property from which maintenance is ordered for an absent person's wife?
A) It must be property that can be sold easily.
B) It must be of the same type as the right being claimed (e.g., food, clothing, dirhams).
C) It must be trade goods only.
D) It can be any property left by the absent person.

Key Takeaway
Ordering maintenance from an absent person's property is limited to wife, child, and parents, from property of the same type as their right, with acknowledgment or judicial knowledge, and includes procedural safeguards of oath and surety.

Maintenance for the Absent Husband: Majority vs. Zufar
Intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance for absent husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position and Zufar's position on maintenance for an absent husband, explain the reasoning behind each, and identify the mufta bihi and its practical consequence.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absentee is forbidden, but necessity later allowed maintenance orders for deserted wives.

In this passage, we explore a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: can a judge impose maintenance on an absent husband who left no property, when the wife brings evidence of marriage? The majority of the early Hanafi masters held that such a judgment is prohibited because it is a judgment against an absentee (qada' ala al-gha'ib). But Imam Zufar, citing necessity, argued for an exception. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the practical consequences, and how later Hanafi practice resolved the conflict.

Guided Path
1
State the context
What is the act being ruled? A wife seeking maintenance from an absent husband who left no property.
2
Identify the majority position
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the judge cannot impose maintenance or rule on marriage because it is qada' ala al-gha'ib.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
3
Identify Zufar's position
Zufar holds that the judge can impose maintenance (but not rule on marriage) and order the wife to borrow on his credit, citing necessity.
Reason: necessity (wives often suffer hardship).
4
Analyze the reasoning
Understand the dalil for each view: majority's principle against absentee judgments vs. Zufar's istihsan based on need.
Note the reference to the twenty issues where later Hanafis deviated.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The mufta bihi is Zufar's view due to necessity. The practical consequence: wives can get court orders for maintenance today.
This is a classic example of darurah overriding a general principle.

ولا إن لم يُخَلّف مالاً فأقامَتْ بيِّنةً عليه ليفرض القاضي عليه، ويأمرها بالاستدانة عليه، ولا يقضي به وقال زفر : يقضي بالنفقة لا بالنكاح (ليفرض القاضي عليه، ويأمرها بالاستدانة عليه، ولا يقضي به) : أي بالنكاح ؛ لأنه قضاء على الغائب، وقال زفر له يقضى بالنفقة لا بالنكاح بالاستقراض عليهما كما مرّ في صورة نفقة زوجة العاجز عن النفقة ؛ وذلك لأن في فرض النفقة والأمر بالاستدانة على الغائب بإقامة البينة قضاء على الغائب، وقبولُ البينة بلا خصم حاضر ، وذلك لا يجوز ، ولذلك لا يقضي بالنكاح أيضاً. : [4] [1] قوله : ويأمرها ؛ بالنصب عطف على يفرض ؛ أي أقامت المرأة بينة ليفرض القاضي نفقتها على الغائب، وليأمرها بالاستدانة عليه ؛ لعدم وجود ماله حتى تستوفي منه. [2] قوله : ولا يقضي ؛ بصيغة المجهول أو بصيغة المعروف، والضمير راجع إلى ما رجع إليه ضمير «يكفلها»، وضمير «يحلفها» وهو القاضي، وهذه الجملة معطوفة على جملة: «لا بإقامة البينة يعني لا تفرضُ النفقة بإقامة البينة على النكاح، سواء ترك مالاً أو لم يترك، ولا يقضي بتلك البينة بثبوت النكاح. [3]قوله : لأنه ؛ أي القضاء بالنكاح ، أو هو وفرض النفقة والأمر بالاستدانة. [4]قوله : وقال زفر له و حاصل مذهبه أنّها إذا أقامت بينة على النكاح ليفرض القاضي النفقة إذا لم يعلمه القاضي أو أنكره المودع وغيره، فحينئذ يلزم على القاضي أن يقضي بهذه البينة بالنفقة عليه، فإن كان له مال تعطى من ماله، وإلا تؤمر بالاستقراض، ولا يقضي بالنكاح. والوجه في ذلك : أنّ القضاء بالنكاح قضاء على الغائب، وهو غير جائز من غير ضرورة، وأما فرض النفقة والأمر بالاستدانة فهو وإن كان أيضاً قضاء على الغائب لكن يحتاج إليه كثيراً ، فإنّ الزوج كثيراً ما يغيب ويتركها بلا نفقة، ولا يعلمُ القاضي ولا غيره نكاحها. فتقبل بينته نظراً لما في حق لزوم النفقة، وليس فيه ضرر على الغائب ؛ لأنه لو حضر وصدقها أو أثبتت ذلك بطريقة كانت آخذة لحقها، وإلا فيرجع الزوج على الزوجة أو على الكفيل الذي يأخذه القاضي فيها عند فرض النفقة. كذا قال الزَّيْلَعِي في «شرح الكنز» (1) (1) تبيين الحقائق (3 : 60). وعمل القضاة اليومَ على هذا للحاجة [1] قوله : على هذا ؛ أي على قول زفر له ، وإن كان خلافُ مذهب مشايخنا الثلاثة للضرورة، وهذا من جملة المسائل العشرين التي أفتوا فيها للحاجة على قول زفر خلاف مذهب أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه . وقد نظمها العلامة محمد أمين المعروف بابن عابدين الشامي المتوفى سنة (1250)(2)، وذكر نظمه في حواشي المتعلقة بـ «الدر المختار» المسماة بـ «رد المحتار»(3) فلتطالع.
And if he left no property, and she presents evidence against him so that the judge imposes upon him, and orders her to borrow on his account, and he does not rule by it. And Zufar said: He rules for maintenance, not for marriage. (So that the judge imposes upon him, and orders her to borrow on his account, and does not rule by it): i.e., by the marriage; because it is a judgment against an absentee. And Zufar said: He rules for maintenance, not for marriage, by borrowing on his account, as was mentioned in the case of maintenance of the wife of the one incapable of maintenance. And that is because in imposing maintenance and ordering borrowing against the absentee by establishing evidence, it is a judgment against an absentee and acceptance of evidence without a present opponent, and that is not permissible. Therefore, he does not rule for marriage either. ... And the judges today act upon this due to necessity. He said: 'Upon this' means upon the saying of Zufar, even though it opposes the madhhab of our three shaykhs due to necessity. And this is among the twenty issues in which they gave fatwa for need according to Zufar's saying, contrary to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa and his two companions. ... So let it be consulted.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a wife presents evidence of marriage to an absent husband who left no property. The base text states that the judge imposes maintenance, orders her to borrow on his credit, but does not rule on the marriage. The commentator explains that 'does not rule by it' refers to the marriage, because it would be a judgment against an absentee, which is not permissible without a present opponent. Zufar holds that the judge should rule for maintenance but not for marriage, citing necessity as wives often suffer hardship. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) disagree because even imposing maintenance is a judgment against an absentee. However, the commentator notes that later judges adopted Zufar's position due to need, listing it among twenty issues where they departed from the three imams' view. The passage concludes with a reference to Ibn Abidin's poem on these issues.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qada' ala al-gha'ib): A legal judgment issued against a person who is not present in court.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses, used to prove a claim in court.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance: food, clothing, and shelter owed by the husband to his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the majority of Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), why does the judge not impose maintenance on an absent husband when the wife presents evidence?
A) Because the husband is absent and cannot defend himself, making it a prohibited judgment against an absentee.
B) Because the wife must first prove the husband has property.
C) Because maintenance is not obligatory in the absence of property.
D) Because evidence of marriage is not acceptable without the husband's acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an absent husband's maintenance cannot be ordered by default, but necessity allowed an exception for deserted wives.

Can the Word 'Talaq' Effect Emancipation?
Dispute with Shafi'i on using talaq for itaq via metaphor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects using the word 'talaq' to effect emancipation, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Metaphor requires a necessary concomitant relationship; talaq is not a necessary result of itaq, so the reverse metaphor fails.

This passage presents a classic inter-madhhab debate: can the word 'talaq' (divorce) be used to effect 'itaq' (emancipation) by way of metaphor? The Shafi'i school argues yes, based on the shared concept of removal of ownership. The Hanafi school responds with a precise definition of metaphor, insisting on a necessary concomitant relationship that is only one-way. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi usul al-fiqh governs analogical reasoning and linguistic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
Identify the central question: can the word 'talaq' be used to mean 'itaq' metaphorically? Then state the Shafi'i and Hanafi positions.
Clarify that the context is a husband speaking to his slave-wife.
2
Understand the Shafi'i proof
The Shafi'i argue that both 'itaq' and 'talaq' involve removal of ownership, so each can be used metaphorically for the other.
They see a general similarity (jins) that permits metaphorical transfer.
3
Identify the Hanafi response
The Hanafis define metaphor as using a word for its necessary concomitant (lazim). They argue that while emancipation necessarily removes marital ownership, divorce does not necessarily remove slave ownership.
Thus the relationship is one-way: itaq can be used for talaq, but not vice versa.
4
Analyse the asymmetry
Examine why emancipation necessitates divorce (freeing a slave-wife ends the marriage), but divorce does not necessitate emancipation (a wife may be divorced and remain a slave).
This asymmetry blocks the reverse metaphor.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: if a man tells his slave-wife 'you are divorced' intending freedom, the Hanafi school says only divorce occurs (if she is his wife), while the Shafi'i school says she becomes free.
This shows the real-world impact of the usul principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الشافعي (رحمه الله) يعتق؛ لأن الإعتاق هو إزالة ملك الرقبة، والطلاق إزالة ملك المتعة، فيجوز إطلاق كل واحد منهما على الآخر مجازاً. قلنا: المجاز لفظ يذكر ويراد به لازمه، وإزالة ملك المتعة لازم لإزالة ملك الرقبة، فإنه إذا أعتق أمته يزول ملك المتعة، ولا لزوم على العكس. الطلاق عن أمثلة الكناية، بل كان عليه أن يذكرها قبل قوله: «وبهذا ابني»، وبالجملة: لا يخلو الكلام هاهنا عن تسامح وتمحل.
[١]قوله: لأنّ... الخ؛ حاصله: أنّ الإعتاق والطلاق متناسبان، فيجوز أن يذكر أحدهما ويراد به الآخر، فكما أن الطلاق يقع بلفظ العتق اتفاقاً، يقع العتق بلفظ الطلاق أيضاً. [٢] قوله: هو إزالة ملك الرقبة؛ ظاهره أنه موضوع له، وهو أولى مما صرحوا به أنه موضوع لإثبات القوّة المخصوصة حيث قالوا: إنّ المعنى اللغوي للطلاق ينبئ عن إزالة الحبس ورفع القيد، يقال: أطلقتُ المسجون إذا خليته، وأطلقت البعير عن عقاله والأسير عن إساره إذا أرسلته، فنقل في الشرع إلى رفع قيد النكاح، فإنّ المرأة قد صارت محبوسة عند الزوج بالنكاح، فترسل به. والمعنى اللغوي للعتاق منبئ عن القوة والغلبة، يقال: عتق الطير إذا قوي، فنقل في الشرع لإثباتِ القوّة المخصوصة من المالكية والشهادة والولاية، وفرعوا على هذا عدم جواز استعارة أحد اللفظين عن الآخر؛ لعدم التشابه من اللفظين، كما حققه الشارح في «التنقيح» و «التوضيح» ويرد عليهم: أنه لو كان الإعتاق عبارة عن إثباتِ القوة المخصوصة لَمّا صح إسناده إلى المالك في مثل: أعتق فلانٌ عبده؛ إذ ليس في وسعهِ إثباتُ القوة بل إزالة ملكِ الرقبة.
(۲) وأجيب عنه تارة بأنه مجاز في الإسناد، وحيث يسند الفعل إلى السبب البعيد، فيجري المجاز من أحد الطرفين، وهو أن يذكر الحرية ويراد بهما الطِّلاقُ لا على العكس. وتارة بأنه مجاز في المسند، حيث يطلق الموضوع لإثبات القوة على سببه الذي هو إزالة الملك. وقال في «التلويح»: «كلا الوجهين ضعيف، إذ لا يفهم من الإعتاق لغةً وعرفاً وشرعاً إلا إزالة الملك والتخليص عن الرق، ولا يصح إسناده حقيقة إلا إلى المالك، وما ذكره من معنى إثباتِ القوّة إنّما يعرفه الأفراد من الفقهاء، فكون اللفظ منقولاً إليه لا إلى إزالة الملك ممنوع لا بدّ من إثباته بنقل وسماع؛ لأنه العمدة في إثبات وضع الألفاظ. وكون إثبات العتق أنسب بمأخذ الاشتقاق، لا يصلح دليلاً على ذلك؛ لجواز أن ينقل اللفظ إلى معنى غيره أنسب بالمعنى الحقيقي، على أنا لا نُسلّم أن الإعتاق منقول، حقيقة لغوية لم يطرأ عليه نقل شرعي». انتهى بل هو.
(1)
[1] قوله: فيجري المجاز...الخ؛ توضيحه: يقتضي تمهيد مقدمات:
الأولى: إن المجاز عبارة عن لفظ يستعمل في غير الموضوع له، ولا بد فيه من العلاقة بين المعنى الأصلي وبين المعنى المجازي؛ ليصح استعمال اللفظ الموضوع للأوّل في الثاني، فإنّه لو لم تعتبر تلك العلاقة يكون ذلك الاستعمال غلطاً، كاستعمال الأرض في السماء. وتلك العلاقة إن كانت علاقة التشبيه فيسمى حينئذ استعارة، كإطلاق الأسد على الإنسان الشجاع؛ لعلاقة اشتراكهما في وصف الشجاعة، وقصد تشبيه المستعار له أي الإنسان الشجاع بالمستعار منه؛ أي الأسد. وإن كانت غير ذلك يسمّى مجازاً مرسلاً، وينقسم المجاز المرسل بحسب تنوّع العلاقات إلى أقسام كثيرة، كإطلاق الجزء على الكلّ، وإطلاق السبب على المسبب، والحال على المحل، وبالعكس إلى غير ذلك مما هو مبسوط في كتب أصول الفقه، وكتب علم المعاني والبيان.
And according to al-Shafi‘i (may Allah have mercy on him) he becomes free; because emancipation is the removal of ownership of the neck (the slave), and divorce is the removal of ownership of enjoyment (mut‘ah), so it is permissible to use each of them metaphorically for the other. We said: metaphor is a word mentioned and intended by it its necessary concomitant. Removal of ownership of enjoyment is a necessary concomitant of removal of ownership of the neck, for when he emancipates his slave-girl, the ownership of enjoyment ceases, but there is no necessity in the opposite direction. Divorce from examples of kinayah? Rather he should have mentioned them before his statement: 'and with this my son'. In summary: this speech is not free from leniency and contrivance.
[1] His statement: 'because...' etc.; its summary: that emancipation and divorce are analogous, so it is permissible to mention one and intend the other. Just as divorce occurs by the word emancipation by agreement, so also emancipation occurs by the word divorce. [2] His statement: 'it is removal of ownership of the neck'; its apparent meaning is that it is the literal meaning (mawdu‘ lahu), and it is more appropriate than what they have stated that it is established for establishing specific power (quwwah makhsusah). They said: the linguistic meaning of talaq indicates removal of confinement and lifting of a restraint. It is said: 'I released the prisoner' when you freed him, and 'I released the camel from its hobble' and 'the captive from his bonds' when you set him free. Then in sacred law it was transferred to the lifting of the marriage bond, for the woman had become confined with the husband through marriage, so she is released by it. The linguistic meaning of ‘itaq indicates strength and overcoming. It is said: 'the bird became strong (‘ataqa)' when it became strong. Then in sacred law it was transferred to establishing specific power of ownership, testimony and guardianship. They derived from this the impermissibility of using one word as a metaphor for the other due to lack of similarity between the two words, as the commentator verified in al-Tanqih and al-Tawdih. It is objected upon them: if ‘itaq were an expression for establishing specific power, then its attribution to the owner in cases like 'so-and-so emancipated his slave' would not be correct, for it is not within his capacity to establish power, rather it is removal of ownership of the neck.
(2) And it was answered sometimes that it is a metaphor in the attribution (majaz fi al-isnad), where the action is attributed to the remote cause. So the metaphor occurs from one side only, which is to mention freedom and intend divorce, not the reverse. And sometimes that it is a metaphor in the predicate (majaz fi al-musnad), where the term established for establishing power is applied to its cause which is removal of ownership. And it is said in al-Talwih: 'Both ways are weak, for nothing is understood from ‘itaq linguistically, customarily and legally except removal of ownership and deliverance from slavery, and its attribution in reality is not correct except to the owner. What he mentioned of the meaning of establishing power is only known by individual jurists. So the claim that the word is transferred to that meaning rather than to removal of ownership is denied, and it must be proven by transmission and hearing, for that is the foundation in proving the assignment of words. And that the establishment of it is more appropriate with the root of derivation does not suffice as proof for that, because it is possible that the word was transferred to another meaning more appropriate with the real meaning. Moreover we do not concede that ‘itaq is transferred; it is a linguistic reality that no legal transfer has occurred to it.' End quote. Rather it is.
(1)
[1] His statement: 'so the metaphor occurs...' etc.; its clarification necessitates laying down premises:
First: Metaphor is a word used in other than its assigned meaning, and there must be a connection (munasabah) between the original meaning and the metaphorical meaning for the use of the word assigned to the first in the second to be correct. For if that connection is not considered, that use would be an error, like using 'earth' for 'sky'. That connection if it is a connection of similarity, then it is called 'simile-metaphor' (isti‘arah), like applying 'lion' to a brave man, due to the connection of sharing the quality of bravery, and intending to liken the metaphorical object (the brave man) to the metaphorical source (the lion). And if it is other than that, it is called 'unrestricted metaphor' (majaz mursal). Unrestricted metaphor is divided according to the diversity of connections into many types, like using the part for the whole, the cause for the effect, the state for the place, and vice versa, etc., which are elaborated in books of usul al-fiqh and books of ma‘ani and bayan.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi‘is regarding whether the word talaq (divorce) can be used to effect emancipation (itaq) by way of metaphor. The Shafi‘i position, as stated here, is that yes, it does effect emancipation because both emancipation and divorce involve removal of ownership: emancipation removes ownership of the slave (raqabah), while divorce removes ownership of marital enjoyment (mut‘ah). Since they share the idea of removal of ownership, each can be used metaphorically for the other. The Hanafi response is that metaphor requires a necessary concomitant relationship, but the relationship is only one-way: emancipation necessarily removes marital enjoyment (since a slave-girl, being a wife, becomes free and thus no longer under marital ownership), but divorce does not necessarily remove ownership of the slave (the wife remains a slave). Therefore, one cannot use talaq to mean itaq. The passage then delves into a deeper linguistic analysis. Some Hanafi scholars, as mentioned, defined itaq as establishing specific power (quwwah makhsusah), which would deny the permissibility of using talaq for itaq due to lack of similarity. However, this definition is critiqued because if itaq were about establishing power, then attributing it to the owner (like 'Zayd emancipated his slave') would be incorrect since the owner does not actually give power; he only removes his ownership. Two attempts to rescue this definition via metaphor in attribution or predicate are presented, but the author of al-Talwih rejects both as weak. He insists that the common understanding of itaq, both linguistically and legally, is simply removal of ownership, and it is not a transferred meaning at all. The lesson concludes with a thorough explanation of the concept of metaphor (majaz) including isti‘arah and majaz mursal, emphasizing that a proper connection is required for valid metaphorical use, which is absent in the reverse direction (talaq to itaq).

Key Terms
الإعتاق (al-i‘taq): Emancipation of a slave; removal of ownership of the slave's person (raqabah).
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; removal of the marital bond (ownership of enjoyment).
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage; using a word in a meaning other than its original assignment with a proper connection (munasabah).
الكناية (al-kinayah): Allusion; indirect expression that hints at the intended meaning without explicit mention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject using the word 'talaq' to effect emancipation (itaq)?
A) Because emancipation requires a specific verbal formula unlike divorce.
B) Because talaq removes only marital ownership, which is not a necessary consequence of emancipation.
C) Because the Shafi‘i school disagrees, so Hanafis reject it by default.
D) Because the word 'talaq' is a kinayah and cannot be used for explicit actions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school insists that metaphorical usage requires a necessary concomitant relationship; thus talaq cannot effect itaq because divorce does not necessitate emancipation.

Daman of Mudabbir and Mu'tiq with a Silent Partner
Explains ruling on daman among co-owners after tadbir and itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on daman among co-owners when one makes a slave mudabbir and another frees him, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on tadbir divisibility.

Core Idea
Tadbir is divisible like itq; daman mu'a'wada takes priority over daman jinaya.

This passage from Imam Abu Hanifa explains a complex scenario of compensation among co-owners of a slave when one performs tadbir and another performs itq. The key is the classification of daman: the mudabbir's compensation is mu'a'wada (exchange) because the slave could transfer ownership, while the mu'tiq's is jinaya (tort). The ruling depends on whether tadbir is divisible—a point of disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
The scenario involves three co-owners: one makes the slave mudabbir, one frees the slave, one remains silent. The passage focuses on the silent partner's claim.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's view on tadbir divisibility
Abu Hanifa holds tadbir is divisible (mutajazzi) like itq, so the tadbir only affects the mudabbir's share. The mudabbir spoils the other shares, but the mu'tiq's act determines his own share.
This determines who owes whom.
3
Classify the types of daman
The daman from tadbir is daman mu'a'wada (the slave was transferable at that time). The daman from itq is daman jinaya (the act of freeing destroys the slave's value as property). Damans are prioritized: mu'a'wada is primary.
4
Determine the flow of claims
The silent partner claims daman mu'a'wada from the mudabbir. Then the mudabbir claims daman jinaya from the mu'tiq for one-third of the slave's value as mudabbir.
The mu'tiq's daman is only after the mudabbir pays.
5
Contrast with the Sahibayn's view
The Sahibayn hold tadbir is not divisible, so the tadbir applies to the entire slave. Hence the silent partner can claim from either the mudabbir or the mu'tiq, and the mudabbir cannot claim from the mu'tiq.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مدبّره لا معتقه، والمدبر معتقَهُ ثُلُثَهُ مُدبّراً لا لما ضَمِنَه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله؛ وذلك لأنَّ التَّدبير متجزى عنده كالإعتاق، فيقتصر على نصيبه، لكنه أفسد نصيب شريكيه، فأحدهما اختار إعتاق حصّتِه، فتعين حقه فيه، فلم يبق له اختيار أمرٍ آخر كالتضمين وغيره، ثُمَّ للساكتِ توجه سببا ضمان: أي ضمان التدبير والإعتاق. لكن ضمانَ التَّدبير ضمانُ المعاوضة، لأنه قابل للانتقال من ملك إلى ملك، وضمان المعاوضة هو الأصل، فيضمن المدير، ثُمَّ للمدير أن يضمن المعتق ثُلُثَ قيمة العبد مدبراً، وقيمة المدبر ثلثا قيمته قناً. أيضاً سبب الضمان، وتقريره: إنَّ ضمان المدبّر ضمان معاوضة، وضمان المعتق ضمانُ جناية وإتلاف، والأصل في الضمان هو ضمان المعاوضة، فلا يعدلُ عنه إلى غيره إلا عند العجز.
His mudabbir, not his mu'tiq, and the mudabbir has against the mu'tiq one-third of his value as mudabbir, not for what he (the mudabbir) had guaranteed. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. That is because tadbir is divisible in his view like itq, so it is confined to his share, but he spoils the share of his two partners. Then one of them chooses to free his share, so his right is determined in it, and he has no other choice like demanding compensation and others. Then for the silent one, two causes of compensation arise: the compensation of tadbir and that of itq. But the compensation of tadbir is a compensation of exchange, because it is capable of transfer from one ownership to another, and the compensation of exchange is the original, so he demands compensation from the mudabbir. Then the mudabbir may demand compensation from the mu'tiq for one-third of the slave's value as a mudabbir, and the value of a mudabbir is two-thirds of his value as a free (qinn) slave. Also the reason for compensation and its explanation: indeed the compensation of the mudabbir is a compensation of exchange, and the compensation of the mu'tiq is a compensation of tort (jinaya) and destruction. The original in compensation is the compensation of exchange, so one does not turn away from it to other than it except in case of inability.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding a scenario where a jointly owned slave is made a mudabbir by one co-owner, then another co-owner sets the slave free, and the third co-owner does nothing. The passage explains the rights of compensation (daman) among them. According to Abu Hanifa, tadbir is divisible (mutajazzi), like itq, so the tadbir only affects the mudabbir's share. However, it renders the shares of the other two partners 'spoiled' (fasid) in the sense that they cannot sell or gift their shares. The partner who frees his share has his right determined, so he can no longer choose other options like demanding compensation. The silent partner has two possible causes of daman: one from the tadbir and one from the itq. However, the daman from tadbir is classified as daman mu'a'wada (compensation of exchange) because at the time of tadbir, the slave was transferable from one ownership to another; thus the mudabbir must compensate the silent partner for spoiling his share. The mudabbir, in turn, can claim daman from the mu'tiq, but only for the value of the mudabbir's share as a mudabbir (i.e., one-third of the slave's value as mudabbir, which is two-thirds of the qinn value). This is because the mu'tiq's act of freeing is a stronger spoilage (jinaya), and the daman from it is daman jinaya (tort), which is secondary to daman mu'a'wada. The passage emphasizes that the original form of daman is mu'a'wada, and one only resorts to jinaya when that is not possible.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by an owner that a slave will be free upon the owner's death.
ضمان معاوضة (daman mu'a'wada): Compensation based on exchange, where the thing compensated was capable of being transferred from one ownership to another at the time of the act.
ضمان جناية (daman jinaya): Compensation for tort or destruction, where the thing compensated was not transferable at the time of the act.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose owner has made him a mudabbir (to be free upon owner's death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the classification of the daman that the silent partner (al-sakit) claims from the mudabbir?
A) Daman jinaya
B) Daman mu'a'wada
C) Daman al-gharama
D) Daman al-kharij

Key Takeaway
The daman from tadbir is mu'a'wada and takes priority; the divisibility of tadbir determines the flow of claims among co-owners.

Dispute on Umm Walad Declared by One Partner
Explains intra-Hanafi dispute on umm walad in joint ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the case of a jointly-owned umm walad claimed by one partner and denied by the other, and explain the underlying basis for the difference in terms of daman tamalluk and taqawwum.

Core Idea
When one co-owner claims a slave is the other's umm walad, Abu Hanifa splits service; the Sahibayn allow the denier to demand istis'a' for half her value.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), concerning a jointly-owned slave girl who one partner claims is the umm walad (mother of his child) of the other partner, who denies it. The ruling hinges on two key concepts: the nature of the guarantee (daman tamalluk vs daman jinaaya) and whether an umm walad is considered valuable (mutaqawwima) for the purpose of compensation. Understanding this dispute deepens your appreciation of how fundamental principles yield different rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and the question
Two partners jointly own a slave girl. One partner says: 'She is the umm walad of my partner.' The other partner denies this. What rights does the denier have over her? Does the acknowledging partner retain any right? Can the slave earn her freedom? This is the core of the dispute.
The dispute is about the outcome for the denier's share of the slave.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and his daman tamalluk reasoning
Abu Hanifa rules that the slave serves the denier one day and stops the next (i.e., she does not serve the acknowledging partner at all). This is because the acknowledging partner's admission is binding on him—he admits he has no right over her. The denier claims full rights over half of her, so he gets half of her service. The guarantee here is daman tamalluk (guarantee of ownership), which is fixed and does not vary with the wealth of the guarantor, unlike daman jinaaya (guarantee of injury) which varies. Therefore, the denier cannot demand istis'a' (earning) because that would be a form of compensation, which is not part of daman tamalluk.
Daman tamalluk contrasts with daman jinaaya, which would allow compensation.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and their istis'a' reasoning
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that the denier can force the slave girl to earn (istis'a') half her value through her work, and upon paying that amount, she becomes free. They consider the umm walad to be valuable (mutaqawwima) because she can be used for service, hiring, and intercourse—even though she cannot be sold. They analogize her to a mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death), who is allowed to earn to buy his freedom. Since the denier loses his share of the slave's value, he has a right to compensation, which takes the form of istis'a'.
The Sahibayn argue that utility, not just saleability, confers value.
4
Analyze the concept of taqawwum and its role in the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that taqawwum (legal existence of value) depends on ihraz (possession for commercial purposes). An umm walad is possessed for the sake of lineage (nasab), not for trade or profit, so she is not mutaqawwima. Therefore, her 'value' cannot be assessed for compensation. The Sahibayn, on the other hand, focus on the actual utility and benefits derived from her (service, hire, etc.), which make her economically valuable. This reflects a deeper usul difference on how value is assessed in property law.
This difference determines whether the denier can claim compensation through istis'a'.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave remains in service half the time indefinitely; she never gains freedom through this process. Under the Sahibayn, the denier can demand that she earn half her value, and once she does, she becomes free. The practical difference is between an ongoing servitude and a path to emancipation. Later Hanafi practice may follow the Sahibayn in some contexts, especially in later fatawa where istis'a' is more widely accepted.
The thamarah al-khilaf affects both the slave's life and the denier's remedy.

(And if he said: she is the umm walad of my partner, and he denied, she serves him one day and stops one day), this is according to Abu Hanifa; because the acknowledge has acknowledged that he has no right over her, so his acknowledgement is taken, then the denier claims that she is as she was, so he has no right over her except in half of her. As for according to them (the two), the denier may make the slave girl earn (by istis'a') half of her value, then she becomes free. ...[continued]
Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where one of two co-owners of a slave girl claims that she is the umm walad (mother of his child, thus free upon his death) of his partner. The partner denies this. The ruling is presented with an intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave girl should serve the denier one day and stop the next (serving neither on alternate days). This is because the acknowledging partner has admitted he has no right over her, so his admission is binding on him. The denier claims full rights over half her, so she serves him half the days. The service is split: she serves the denier one day, then does not serve either the next day (because the acknowledger's half is tied up by his admission). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the denier can force the slave girl to earn (istis'a') half her value by working and then she becomes free. They analogize this to the case of a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) where the slave is valuable and can earn. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that the guarantee here is a guarantee of ownership (daman tamalluk), which does not vary with the wealth of the guarantor, unlike a guarantee of emancipation (which is like a guarantee of injury). The commentary also discusses the fundamental principle: according to Abu Hanifa, an umm walad has no legal value (ghayr mutaqawwima) because she is reserved for the father's lineage and not for commerce. The Sahibayn hold she is valuable because she can be used for service, hiring, and intercourse. This difference affects whether the denier can claim compensation or force her to earn. The passage ends with the beginning of the argument on taqawwum.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thereby becoming free upon his death.
استسعاء (istis'a'): A process where a slave is allowed to earn money to purchase their freedom, often by working for others.
ضمان تملك (daman tamalluk): A guarantee that results in obtaining ownership of some benefit (e.g., service, earnings).
تقوم (taqawwum): The legal quality of having a recognized monetary value in the market.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She is sold and the proceeds are divided.
D) The denier can make her earn half her value.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling on alternating service for a jointly-owned umm walad is based on daman tamalluk and the non-valuable status of an umm walad, contrasting with the Sahibayn's allowance of istis'a'.

Freeing a Slave Girl as Dower: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dower for freed slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on dower when a slave girl is freed on condition of marriage, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding freeing as dower.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave is not property, so it cannot be a valid dower – the Prophet's case was unique to him.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya presents a complex case involving conditional emancipation and marriage. It clarifies the division of payment when a man offers to free a slave girl for a sum on condition that her master marries her to him. The text then records a key intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf allows freeing as a dower, citing the Prophet's marriage to Safiyya, while the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require the customary dower (mahr al-mithl). Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between general rulings and the Prophet's special prerogatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling (refusal case)
Start with the master's offer: 'Free your slave girl for me for a thousand, on condition that you marry her to me.' If the slave girl refuses, divide the payment proportionally between her value (to the master) and her mahr al-mithl (returned to the man).
The example: value=1000, mahr al-mithl=500, total 1500 → 2/3 for value, 1/3 for mahr.
2
Understand the division when she refuses
The man pays the share equal to the value (e.g., 666.66) to the master, and the share corresponding to mahr al-mithl (333.33) is dropped because the marriage did not occur.
This is the earlier scenario; the passage then shifts to when she consents.
3
Recognize the ruling when she marries
If the slave girl marries the man, her dower is the share of mahr al-mithl from the payment (333.33). The value share goes to the master. The phrase 'in both aspects' covers cases where the man said 'for me' or 'from me' – both same ruling.
The dower becomes mahr al-mithl, not the freeing itself.
4
Analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute on freeing as dower
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: freeing cannot be dower because it is not property (mal); mahr al-mithl is due. Abu Yusuf: freeing can be dower, citing the Prophet's freeing of Safiyya and making it her dower. The majority responds that this was a special prerogative (khususiyyah) of the Prophet.
The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is the majority view.

Book of Emancipation: The oath by emancipation and emancipation for a consideration. If she marries, then her share of the dower is her dower in both aspects. That is, if he said, 'Free your slave girl for me for a thousand,' and the rest of the case remains as it is, then the emancipation occurs on behalf of the commander by way of constructive meaning, as you know. So the thousand is divided between her value and her mahr al-mithl. We assume her value is a thousand and her mahr al-mithl is five hundred, so the thousand is divided by a thousand and five hundred: two-thirds of the thousand is the share of the value, one-third is the share of the mahr al-mithl. So he must pay two-thirds of the thousand to the master, and one-third is dropped from him, because he exchanged the thousand for the slave's person by purchase and for her sexual organ by marriage. The person was secured for him, but not the sexual organ, so he must pay the share of what was secured for him, and not the share of what was not. [0] (If she marries, then her share of the dower is her dower in both aspects) i.e. if she marries, the amount corresponding to the mahr al-mithl is dropped from him. [1] His saying 'as it is' means he said 'on condition that you marry her to me' after saying 'free your slave girl for me for a thousand,' and the master did as ordered, but the slave girl refused marriage. In this scenario, mentioning the word 'on condition' or not mentioning it is the same. [2] His saying 'on behalf of the commander' because his speech implies effecting purchase and emancipation as an agent. [3] His saying 'as you know' meaning previously in the discussions of divorce. [4] His saying 'share of the value' because two-thirds of a thousand and five hundred, which is the sum of value and dower, is the thousand, which is the amount of the value. [0] His saying 'if she marries... etc.' — in Kashf al-Wiqaya: This that we have mentioned is only on the assumption of refusal. But if she does not refuse and marries him, then her dower is the share of mahr al-mithl from the thousand, which is one-third of the thousand in our example. He said in al-Tabyin: 'If she married herself to him in both aspects, he did not mention it in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The answer: what corresponds to her value is dropped in the first aspect, and belongs to the master in the second aspect; what corresponds to her mahr al-mithl is her dower in both aspects. And if he freed a slave girl on condition that she marry him, and she married him, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad she is entitled to her mahr al-mithl, because emancipation is not property, so it cannot be a valid dower. At Abu Yusuf, he permits making emancipation a dower, because the Prophet freed Safiyya and married her, and made her emancipation her dower. We say: That was specific to the Prophet for marriage without a dower. And if she refuses to marry him, then she must pay her value, according to all of them.' End. [1] His saying 'this that we have mentioned' means her emancipation without anything being owed by the commander in the first aspect, and her emancipation with the obligation of the value's share on the commander, and dropping the share of the mahr al-mithl.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya explains a complex case in Hanafi fiqh. The main text (matn) says: 'If she marries, then her share of the dower is her dower in both aspects.' The commentary expands: The case is where a man says to a slave owner, 'Free your slave girl for me for a thousand, on condition that you marry her to me.' If the slave girl refuses (the initial scenario discussed earlier), the payment of one thousand is divided proportionally between her value (which goes to the master) and her mahr al-mithl (the customary dower, which the man gets back because the marriage didn't happen). In the example, if her value is 1000 and mahr al-mithl is 500, the total is 1500, so 2/3 of the 1000 goes to value (i.e., 666.66) and 1/3 goes to mahr al-mithl (333.33). The man must pay the 2/3 to the master, and the 1/3 is dropped because the marriage didn't occur. Now, the ruling changes if the slave girl consents and marries: in that case, her dower is the share of mahr al-mithl from the thousand (333.33), and the value portion is paid to the master. The phrase 'in both aspects' refers to two scenarios: (1) where the man says 'free your slave girl for me' (without specifying 'from me') and (2) where he says 'free your slave girl from me' — both have the same ruling. The passage then records an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say that freeing a slave cannot be a valid dower because it is not property (mal), so the woman is entitled to mahr al-mithl. Abu Yusuf, however, allows it, citing the hadith that the Prophet freed Safiyya and made her emancipation her dower. The Hanafi response is that this was a special prerogative (khususiyyah) of the Prophet, not generalizable. The passage concludes that if she refuses to marry, she must pay her value according to all three imams.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower of a woman in her family, used when the actual dower is not specified or invalid.
العتق (al-‘itq): The act of freeing a slave, which is a form of emancipation.
الصداق (al-sadaq): The dower given by the husband to the wife in marriage; synonymous with mahr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a man frees a slave girl on condition that she marries him, and she does, what is her dower?
A) Her value, because she is freed.
B) Mahr al-mithl, because freeing is not property.
C) Whatever the man specifies, even if zero.
D) Nothing, because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, freeing a slave cannot be a valid dower because it is not property; the woman is entitled to mahr al-mithl, and the Prophet's precedent was specific to him.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Emancipation as Dower
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of emancipation as mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority position that emancipation cannot be made a dower and distinguish it from Abu Yusuf's view using the concept of khususiyyah.

Core Idea
The Prophet's emancipation of Safiyyah as her dower was a special permission (khususiyyah), not a general ruling.

This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: can a man emancipate a slave-woman and make that emancipation her mahr? The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) say no, while Abu Yusuf says yes, citing the Prophet's marriage to Safiyyah. The key to understanding the majority's position is the doctrine of khususiyyah: certain acts of the Prophet were unique to him and cannot be analogized to others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority Hanafi position
State clearly that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, making emancipation the dower is invalid. The marriage is sahih, but the woman receives mahr al-mithl instead.
The passage assumes a specific scenario where a man orders emancipation and then marries the woman.
2
Identify the dissenting view of Abu Yusuf
Abu Yusuf holds that it is permissible to make emancipation the dower, based on the Prophet's action with Safiyyah.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; Abu Yusuf is one of the two Sahibayn.
3
Analyse Abu Yusuf's proof (dalil naqli)
He cites the hadith that the Prophet emancipated Safiyyah and made her emancipation her mahr. He argues this establishes general permissibility.
This is a direct textual proof from the Sunnah.
4
Explain the Hanafi response (khususiyyah)
The majority respond that the Prophet's action was a special permission (khususiyyah) unique to him, not a general ruling. They support this with other evidence that the Prophet was permitted to marry without a mahr.
Khususiyyah restricts the application of the proof to the Prophet alone.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Yusuf, the emancipation is the mahr. Under the majority, the emancipation is not the mahr; instead, the woman gets mahr al-mithl, and the man is not discharged from his mahr obligation by freeing her.
The difference affects the financial rights of the wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الذي ذكرنا إنما هو على تقدير الإباء، أما إذا لم تاب ونُكِحَت، فمهرها مهر المثل من الألف، وهو ثُلُثُ الألف فيما فرضناه، وقوله: في وجهيه: أي حصة فيما لم يقل: عنِّي، وفيما قال: عنى وعند أبي يوسف يجوز جعل العتق صداقاً؛ لأنه لا أعتق صفية ونكحها، وجعل عتقها مهرها. قلنا: كان النبيل مخصوصاً بالنكاح بغير مهر، فإن أبت أن تزوجه فعليها قيمتها في قولهم جميعاً. انتهى. قوله: هذا الذي ذكرنا؛ أي عتقها بلا وجوب شيء على الآمر في الوجه الأول، وعتقها مع وجوب حصة القيمة على الآمر، وسقوط حصة مهر المثل.
This which we mentioned is only on the assumption of refusal. But if she did not repent and was married, then her dower is the dower of the like from the thousand, and it is one-third of the thousand in what we assumed. And his saying: 'In his two faces': i.e., a share in that which he did not say: 'from me', and in that which he said: 'from me.' And according to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible to make the emancipation a dower, because the Prophet emancipated Safiyyah and married her, and made her emancipation her dower. We said: The Prophet was specifically entitled to marriage without a dower, and if she refuses to marry him, then she must pay her value according to all of them. End. His saying: 'This which we mentioned'; i.e., her emancipation without any obligation on the commander in the first aspect, and her emancipation with the obligation of a share of the value on the commander, and the dropping of the share of the dower of the like.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage deals with an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of making a slave-girl's emancipation the dower (mahr) in marriage. The author first discusses the scenario where a man orders the emancipation of a woman (perhaps under compulsion) and then marries her. If the woman does not repent (i.e., she acquiesces?) and is married, her dower is mahr al-mithl (the proper dower), which in this assumed example is one-third of a thousand. The commentator then explains the phrase 'in his two faces' referring to two different expressions used by the man. Next, he presents the view of Abu Yusuf, who permits making emancipation the dower, citing the Prophet's marriage to Safiyyah (may Allah be pleased with her) where he emancipated her and made that her dower. The text responds ('qulna') that the Prophet was specially permitted (khususiyyah) to marry without a dower, and this is not generalizable. Thus, the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that emancipation cannot be made a dower; rather, the woman is entitled to mahr al-mithl. The concluding commentary clarifies the two aspects: in the first aspect, the woman is freed without any payment by the commander; in the second, the commander must pay a share of the value, and the share of mahr al-mithl is dropped.

Key Terms
صداق (sadaq): The dower or mahr given to the wife in marriage.
عتق (itq): Emancipation from slavery.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower: a fair dower determined by the woman's social status and similar women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling if a man emancipates a slave-woman and marries her, intending the emancipation as her dower?
A) The marriage is invalid because the dower is invalid.
B) The marriage is valid but she is entitled to mahr al-mithl, not the emancipation.
C) The marriage is valid and the emancipation is her dower as per Abu Yusuf.
D) The marriage is valid but the woman must pay back her value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, making emancipation the dower is invalid (ghayr sahih) because the Prophet's action was a khususiyyah; the wife receives mahr al-mithl.

Claiming a Child from a Mukatab's Slave Woman: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on claiming child from mukatab's slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming a child from a mukatab's slave woman and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the need for the mukatib's confirmation.

Core Idea
The master's lack of full ownership over the mukatib's acquisitions prevents automatic lineage establishment without the mukatib's consent.

This lesson examines a nuanced case in family law: when a master claims paternity of a child born to his mukatib's slave woman. The Hanafi school agrees on the financial consequences but splits on whether the master's mere claim suffices or requires the mukatib's confirmation. We will analyze the ruling, the underlying qiyas, and the practical difference between the two positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
Recognize that the act is a master claiming a child born to a slave woman owned by his mukatib (a slave under contract of manumission). This involves three parties: master, mukatib, and the slave woman.
The mukatib is a slave but owns his acquisitions.
2
State the basic ruling from the matn
The master must pay 'uqr (compensation for intercourse). The child's lineage is established, but the child must pay his own value to be free. The slave woman does not become umm walad because the master has no real ownership over her.
This is conditional upon the mukatib's confirmation (according to Abu Hanifa).
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the mukatib to confirm the master's claim. Abu Yusuf holds that the master's mere claim suffices, by analogy to a father claiming a child from his son's slave woman.
The dispute centers on the nature of the master's right over the mukatib's property.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the father-son case: just as a father's claim over his son's slave woman establishes lineage without the son's confirmation, so should the master's claim over the mukatib's slave woman suffice. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the father owns his son's earnings, but the master does not own the mukatib's acquisitions.
The effective cause (illa) is ownership of the other party's property, which is absent in the master-mukatib relationship.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: if the mukatib denies, the lineage is not established unless the master later acquires the child (by purchase or gift). Under Abu Yusuf: the lineage is established immediately upon the master's claim, regardless of the mukatib's denial.
The exception: if the master later owns the child, the obstacle of the mukatib's right is removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ادعى ولد أمة مكاتبة لزمه عقرها ، ونسب الولد وقيمته لا الأمة إن صدقه مكاتبه.
(وإن ادعى ولد أمة مكاتبة لزمه عقرها ) ، ونسب الولد وقيمته)؛ لأنه وطئ معتمداً على الملك، فيكون ولده ولد المغرور ، وهو ثابت النسب، وهو حرّ بالقيمة، (لا الأمة : أي لا تصير الأمة أم ولد له إذ لا ملك له فيها حقيقة، (إن صدقه مكاتبه ) : أي إنّما يثبتُ النَّسب إن صدق المكاتب المولى، وإلا لا يثبت نسبه إلا إذا ملكهماً
وعند أبي يوسف لا يشترط تصديقُ المكاتب المولى، وإلا لا يثبت نسبه إلا إذا ملكه ) : أي إن لم يُصدِّق المكاتب المولّى لا يثبت النسب إلا إذا ملك المولى الولد يوماً ) . والله أعلم.
[١ ] قوله : لا يشترط ؛ بل يثبتُ النَّسب بمجرد دعوة المولى، كما في الأب ؛ وذلك لان جارية المكاتب كسب كسب المولى، كما أنّ جارية الابن كسب كسب الأب، فيثبت النسب في الصورتين بلا اعتبار تصديق المالك وتكذيبه.
لنا : إنَّ المولى لا يملك التصرف في اكتساب المكاتب، ولا يمتلكه عند الحاجة، والأب يملكه ، فلا يشترط تصديق الابن، ويشترط تصديق المكاتب.
[٢] قوله : إلا إذا ملكه ؛ أي لو ملك أبو الوالد الذي ادعاه ولد الجارية المذكورة منه من غير اعتبار تصديق العبد المكاتب ؛ لزوال المانع وهو حق المكاتب.
يوماً ثبت نسبه من (١) لقيام الموجب وزوال حق المكاتب. ينظر : «البحر» (٤ : ٣٠٠).
And if he claims a child of a mukatib's slave woman, the 'aqr (compensation) of her is incumbent upon him, and the lineage of the child and his value (is established), but not the slave woman (becoming umm walad) if the mukatib confirms him. (And if he claims a child of a mukatib's slave woman, the 'aqr of her is incumbent upon him), and the lineage of the child and his value; because he had intercourse relying on ownership, so his child is the child of the deceived (walad al-maghrur), and his lineage is established, and he is free with payment of value. (Not the slave woman: i.e., the slave woman does not become umm walad for him since he has no ownership in her in reality. (If the mukatib confirms him): i.e., the lineage is only established if the mukatib confirms the master, otherwise his lineage is not established unless he acquires him). And according to Abu Yusuf, the confirmation of the mukatib by the master is not a condition; otherwise, his lineage is not established unless he acquires him (i.e., if the mukatib does not confirm the master, the lineage is not established unless the master owns the child for a day). And Allah knows best. [1] His saying: not a condition; rather the lineage is established merely by the master's claim, as in the case of the father; and that is because the slave woman of the mukatib is the acquisition (kasb) of the master, just as the slave woman of the son is the acquisition of the father, so lineage is established in both cases without considering the confirmation or denial of the owner. For us: the master does not have the right to dispose of the acquisitions of the mukatib, nor does he own them in case of need, while the father does own them; therefore confirmation of the son is not required, but confirmation of the mukatib is required. [2] His saying: unless he acquires him; i.e., if the father of the claimant who claimed the child from the mentioned slave woman acquires him (the child) for a day, his lineage is established from him without considering the confirmation of the slave mukatib; because the obstacle (the right of the mukatib) has ceased.
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' explains the ruling when a master claims a child born to the slave woman of his mukatib (a slave who has made a contract of manumission). The passage presents the ruling as stated in the matn (al-Wiqaya) and then the sharh expands with proofs and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The passage first states the ruling: if a master claims a child from a mukatab's slave woman, he must pay the 'aqr (compensation for intercourse) and the child's lineage is established, but the child must pay his own value to become free, and the slave woman does not become umm walad (mother of a child of her master) because the master has no real ownership over her. However, all this is conditional upon the mukatib confirming the master's claim. If the mukatib does not confirm, the lineage is not established unless later the master acquires the child through ownership (e.g., the mukatib buys the child). Then the passage notes that according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the mukatib's confirmation is not required; rather, the master's mere claim suffices, just as a father's claim over his son's slave woman suffices even without the son's confirmation. The passage then gives the Hanafi position: the master does not have the same rights over the mukatib's acquisitions as a father has over his son's acquisitions; the father owns his son's earnings, but the master does not own the mukatib's earnings. Therefore, the mukatib's confirmation is necessary. If the mukatib confirms, it indicates his consent to the master's intercourse. The passage also addresses the exception: if the master later owns the child (by the mukatib giving it to him or by purchase), then the lineage is established even without the mukatib's initial confirmation, because the obstacle (the mukatib's right) is removed. The reference to 'Al-Bahr' indicates the source for the fuller discussion.

Key Terms
أمة مكاتبة (amatun mukātabah): A female slave who has entered into a contract of manumission (kitabah) with her master, whereby she will be freed upon paying a sum.
عقر ('uqr): A fixed compensation paid to the master of a slave woman for sexual intercourse, equivalent to the dower of a free woman of her status.
ولد المغرور (walad al-maghrūr): A child born to a man who mistakenly believed he had a valid marriage or ownership, but in reality did not; the child is free and owes his value to the true owner.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to her master's child; she becomes free upon the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for the master's claim to a child from a mukatab's slave woman to establish lineage?
A) The master's mere claim is sufficient.
B) The child must be owned by the master at the time of birth.
C) The mukatib must confirm the master's claim.
D) The slave woman must admit the master's paternity.

Key Takeaway
The master's claim to a child from a mukatab's slave woman requires the mukatib's confirmation; without it, lineage is not established unless the master later acquires the child, due to the master's lack of ownership over the mukatib's acquisitions.
